---
title: "The bet is on!  Ubuntu vs. Microsoft"
date: 2007-10-24
forum: The Cafe
---

### Post by professor fate on 2007-10-24
Hello,
I am here because of a bet I have with a friend.  This is the deal: I have to use Ubuntu for 30 days.  IF there is anything I can’t do on Ubuntu (except for games which he reluctantly conceded is Microsoft turf) that I can do on Vista, then I get a brand new laptop courtesy of my buddy.  IF on the other hand, I lose, I have to pay for his new laptop.  

The rules:
1. I have to post any and all questions I have about installing and using Ubuntu on this forum.
2. I have to save all of my correspondence.  	
3. I cannot use any other OS for 30 days, except for my computer at work.
4. If I can demonstrate that Ubuntu isn’t neck to neck with Microsoft in anyway, I win.
5. I have to use open source software as much as possible.  If there is no equivalent, then I get to use MS software, but using some sort of emulation software to run it on.  (Buddy said I’d have to learn about it here).
6. My friend wanted me to use Feisty, but I told him to step up to the plate, so it’s going to be Gutsy.  (I figure since it’s new, it must have lots of issues, so the better for me).

Well, there it is.  I plan on winning this bet, but I will be fair.  All of this will start tomorrow.  Tonight is the last evening of my beloved Microsoft for 30 days.  I am downloading Gutsy and will install tomorrow. 

Professor Fate

---

### Post by 3rdalbum on 2007-10-24
I'm not trying to insult you, but it sounds like a rather dumb bet - he's using an operating system that he's familiar with, and you're going to be using one that you've never used before. Good luck, though, and I hope you win.

---

### Post by professor fate on 2007-10-24
This is the deal.  I\'m a Microsoft guy..and he\'s a Linux guy.  For months he\'s been going on and on about how Linux can do everything Microsoft can.  I told him to put his money where his mouth is or shut the **** up. Well, now we got some money on the table, or I should say a laptop. 

Now it\'s up to me to drop Microsoft for 30 days and live on a Linux diet.  As I said in my first post, I\'ll be fair and give it a sporting chance.

### Post by sloggerkhan on 2007-10-24
How nitpicky do you plan on being? Are you going to be like "I win because open office doesn't have grammar check, only spell check" (which I tend to view as a feature) ?  And what about stuff that a "Linux Guru" could do, but you probably can't learn in a month?

It seems like a weird bet unless you are both fairly liberal in interpretation or have a really detailed contract. 

That said, if you view things by type of task, he's probably right. But some of the solutions for those tasks won't be on equal footing between platforms.

---

### Post by DoctorMO on 2007-10-24
In one sense he's bribing you to use ubuntu and concede that it's a viable platform to do productive work (which it is) on the other foot despite the Microsoft paradime of complete and utter indifference to users, Microsoft have got an awful lot of money to bribe, trick and generally act quite immoral to squew the markets and this is something a lot of Linux users have come to terms with. We here accept that the market is designed for windows users at the moment and it's our sheer tenacity and cleverness that allows us as a community to work arround the windows industry momentum.

We also have a saying: "Linux is not Windows" and you know it's not, it does different things, some things it doesn't need and some things it can't do. So if your bet is about core operational functionality then you will loose but if it's about all the apps, games, drivers and various other things you might want to use then you will run into the above problem of working against the momentum with absolutely no incentive to grind away problems with the community.

Still it'll give you a good view of ubuntu and a free laptop; although if it was me I would have stipulated that the laptop much always run ubuntu, but that's because I'm a bar stuard.

### Post by dasunst3r on 2007-10-24
I use Ubuntu 99% exclusively and am preparing to virtualize Windows XP Professional altogether at the end of the semester.

I will personally follow this thread to keep myself informed and to help serve as a neutral third party.  Since this is pretty fresh, here's a few observations and words of wisdom:[LIST]
[*]Your friend seems to have made the mistake of not going through your hardware to make sure they work well with Linux.  Before I let any prospective convert make the leap, I make sure their hardware works well with Linux as it stands and *demonstrate* that it does.
[*]Even if you found something that Ubuntu can't do, *please* stick to it for the entire 30-day stint for the sake of self-enlightenment.[/LIST]In any case, welcome to the forums and thanks for giving Ubuntu a fair shot.  Search before posting a new question. :)

### Post by jpittack on 2007-10-24
I would love to know what you do in Vista on your day to day basis.  I would also like to know how you feel about the general desktop environment.  Mainly compiz fusion if you decide to use that.  Or if workspaces have a benefit to you.

A lot of programs stop responding, Adobe was difficult to install because of the new administrator rules (I did not know about them), and I have trouble navigating the folders.

Did you use IE for your main browser in Vista?

I'm sure I will have more questions for you later.

I made my choices because I couldn't stand by and support a company that is actively trying to destroy the IT industry in a bid to own it all; yes they are foolish, have painted themselves into a very difficult position but that doesn't change the fact that the people in that company don't deserve my support, help or even my passive grudging acceptance.

It _does_ make me sad that other people are more practical than moral or that they selectively view the world in a very interesting and selective way unprepared to give up things for the sake of doing a good thing in and of it's self.

### Post by GSF1200S on 2007-10-24
Your friend is going to lose on a bet that isnt fair to him. You certainly are not going to have the tenacity that he would have- youve already admitted that you love Vista, and you are placing money on the fact that Ubuntu cant compete? Would a person who makes such an unfair bet to a friend seriously provide hours of dedication to make something work for his friend? Or would he just cry foul until his friend stepped in and fixed it for him?

He could come there and setup compiz fusion. He could setup application launchers for every application on your gnome panel. He could set up conky and gdesklets. He could setup devils pie to manage which applications open in which windows. He could setup KDE, XFCE, FLUXBOX, icewm, openbox and every window manager under the sun for you to select at the login screen. He could put in custom icon themes, turn your mouse pointer into a silver arrow like mine. He could setup openoffice in a few clicks and the gimp saving you hundreds of dollars.

And.. if you manage to find one thing that windows can do, because it maintains **85% (!)** of the market share versus what OSS has accomplished with .8%, you win.

I really understand where your friend is going with this. Hes trying to open your eyes. He knows you could be a dick and remain a slave to the commercial world, and net a laptop from it. But hes trying to get you in this world, and show you the options hes so passionate about, and the freedom that follows. Hes willing to hold the  carrot of a new laptop in front of you to get you to try it, knowing full well if you dont "see", hell be giving you one.

The really sad part is.. i bet he could care less whether he gets a shiny new laptop- he just wants to see his friend find the world he s in...

---

### Post by PartisanEntity on 2007-10-24
I think this is a very interesting idea and I look forward to reading about your experiences. However, like others mentioned, you might not have the interest or the internal drive to solve certain problems.

For example when I first started using Ubuntu a year ago, it took me at least a week or two and several reinstallations to get my wifi card working. I had no idea what I was doing and I managed to mess up my system several times (at least at the time I *thought* I had messed it up, today I know I could have fixed it) and I tried out so many How-To's that I nearly lost track of them.

So that's the question, do you have the internal drive to be willing to sit down and try out perhaps 3-4 different tutorials to get a certain piece of hardware working if it happens to not work out of the box?

p.s. your thread was moved because it was not a support request. The beginner forum is for actual support requests, not for general discussions.

### Post by dasunst3r on 2007-10-24
> **JacobRogers said:**
> Here's a quick way to win the bet
See if your laptop can successfully suspend.  Mine can't...
The main culprit of that is usually the graphics card.  Try this: [http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?p=3523957#post3523957](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?p=3523957#post3523957)

Back when I had openSUSE 2.5 years ago, I made a bet with a friend in which I would suspend my laptop and attempt to wake it up.  If it works, I give him $10; if it doesn't, he gives me $10.  I have never seen it work back then, so I was quite sure it won't work now.  I hit suspend, waited 10 seconds, and powered the machine back on.  Sure enough, the machine woke up.  My friend called off the bet as I was reaching for my wallet to give him his $10.

---

### Post by sicofante on 2007-10-24
I understand the bet is about doing with Ubuntu what the guy usually does with Windows. Restore? Really? I tried it in Gutsy just a few days ago, and it couldn't be done.

Or do you mean cut and paste to another location? That's not the same as restore. Cut and paste requires you to remember where it used to be or not care where it used to be. Restore just magically puts it back where it was originally deleted from.

### Post by josephus on 2007-10-24
> 
A couple of times I noticed the CPU shoot up to 90% usage (via the system monitor) though I had nothing running.  Brought the system to a crawl

You can check to see which process is eating up the cpu by clicking on the system monitor.  My first guess would be trackerd - it's a program that indexes your desktop and is known to use the cpu when the system is at idle until it completes the indexing.  You can choose to turn it off if you want.

> **professor fate said:**

As has been said, you need to install the Adobe version of flash, it is very stable in comparison. You can find it in add/remove even after you have enabled universe repos.

Sound is tricky, but this is probably the best place to start:
[https://help.ubuntu.com/community/SoundTroubleshooting?highlight=%28sound%29](https://help.ubuntu.com/community/SoundTroubleshooting?highlight=%28sound%29)

The CPU usage I would bet is something new included in Gutsy. They included a fancy new desktop search function for some reason. It wants to index everything on your computer and can take up a lot of CPU. If you don't use it, then I recommend removing it. Just go into add/remove and find it and remove it. The package is called tracker, but it may be called "Desktop Search" or something else 'nicer' in add/remove. I don't know why they felt this should be included by default, but removing it is easy, so its not a big deal.

As for you new and most critical problem, I've never heard of something so strange. I'll keep looking for a solution.

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-10-24
> **rorestuff said:**
> However, for the most part, I use Ubuntu for everything. It's faster, and never crashes.
This actually appears a little ironic in light of the OP's current problem, no? 

While it's true that the base GNU/Linux system is among the most stable OSes ever, the X11 GUIs aren't. If they're well-configured, they'll crash very rarely, much like a well-configured Win XP system will crash very rarely. 

I use Linux for everything I do, so I'm obviously fond of it, but I don't think it helps to make unrealistic claims for it.

Second, I'm actually surprised by how many popups get through.  If you have suggestions for stopping them, let me know.  It's sort of funny in a disturbing way how FF will notify me that it just blocked popups, yet I'll be looking at ads for registry cleaners, etc., that just popped up.  LOL.  Third, there were a few times that the CPU just flipped out and sucked up tons of processing cycles, but there were no apps running.  I was told it may be part of some indexing system new to Gutsy.  Vista does the same thing, but I don't know how to prevent it in Ubuntu.  Suggestions?

So...that's about it.  Tomorrow sound is on the agenda.

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-10-24
To stop the popups altogether:
```
sudo apt-get install mozilla-noscript
```
This installs a tool that prevents all scripts (JavaScript, XSS, Flash) from running without your permission. There will be a small "S" in the bottom right-hand corner of your browser that you can use to configure how it behaves.

Some people find this application to be more trouble than it's worth. Others (like me) love the control it offers over how the browser behaves.

---

### Post by sloggerkhan on 2007-10-25
For reference, Gutsy does indeed include a .pdf printer. To enable it, :
```

System>Admin>Printing
Click 'New Printer'
Select 'Print into PDF file'
Click 'forward'
Choose 'Generic'
Keep 'PDF file generator' set at the model. Click next.
Name whatever you like. Click apply.

Open program.
File > Print > [Whatever you named your pdf printer here]
Click print. A file with the name [your document name].pdf will be created in the folder 
/home/[your username]/PDF


```

Printer options allow you to print it on various paper sizes and at various DPIs.
This works from any program using the regular print dialog. (As an example, GIMP uses the guttenprint interface, rather than cups by default, so gimp may not see the .pdf printer.) (It will work with inkscape so long as you leave pdf printer as the default printer.) Some apps, such as the default image/document viewer (eye of gnome) will have dialogs with a print to pdf that lets you choose the print to location instead of printing to the .pdf folder.

Here's the thread printed to .pdf:
[http://upload2.net/page/download/VqK3x9uINovwbyb/Nicole.mp3.html](http://upload2.net/page/download/VqK3x9uINovwbyb/Nicole.mp3.html)
[http://www.filecrunch.com/file/~cmkv2a](http://www.filecrunch.com/file/~cmkv2a)
Both are annoying sites, but the forums only let you post pdf up to 20kb or something stupidly small.

---

```
sudo apt-get install build-essential

```
cd ~/Desktop/alsa-driver-*
./configure
make
sudo make install
```Reboot (or restart ALSA sound server).

### Post by Eric the Grey on 2007-10-26
An important option that some people seem to overlook:

Alt+F2 keys open up the Gnome version of the Windows Run command.

Type in xkill and press enter.  Your mouse cursor will turn into an X.  Click on a non-responsive application and it'll just go away.  A nice alternative to rebooting, or restarting the GUI.


:cool: Eric the Grey

### Post by JoeLinux117 on 2007-10-26
Well, I've personally seen the same problem with the ICH8 family of Intel sound cards.  They only come on the newest laptops, and really Ubuntu shouldn't be to blame for the lack of support for that sound card, it's the 3rd parties.  They only make drivers for Windows by default, and ignore everyone else (although this is slowly changing).

However, as for your sound card, I do know that Gutsy no longer uses ESD, but there is a driver that you can install from Alsa.  I've gotten that exact same sound card working on another laptop that had the same problem.

Alsa (a Linux sound project), is working diligently on this, and they have some developmental drivers for the ICH8 family of sound cards.

There are 4 things that you need to do in order to get this working:

1) Compile and install alsa-utils
2) Compile and install alsa-lib
3) Compile and install alsa-driver
4) Run alsaconf

I believe the final release of Alsa's driver will be on Nov 16.  But for now, you can install the development versions of this driver (which works just fine on this other laptop).  Just for argument's sake, the laptop that I got this working on has the 82801H (ICH8 family) Intel sound card.

You may download all three of these files from [http://alsa-project.org](http://alsa-project.org), and since I'm a nice guy (remember that just about everyone in the Linux community is willing and ready to help), I'll even post these on my site temporarily so you can download the exact ones that I used, directly from me:

[http://www.learnaboutlinux.net/files/alsa-driver-1.0.15rc3.tar.bz2]("http://www.learnaboutlinux.net/files/alsa-driver-1.0.15rc3.tar.bz2")
[http://www.learnaboutlinux.net/files/alsa-lib-1.0.15rc3.tar.bz2]("http://www.learnaboutlinux.net/files/alsa-lib-1.0.15rc3.tar.bz2")
[http://www.learnaboutlinux.net/files/alsa-utils-1.0.15rc1.tar.bz2]("http://www.learnaboutlinux.net/files/alsa-utils-1.0.15rc1.tar.bz2")

In case you're not familiar with compiling, let's go through it!

First "cd" into the directory where these three files are located:

```
$ cd /home/whoever_you_are/Downloads
```

Next extract the alsa-utils file first:

```
$ tar jxvf alsa-utils-1.0.15rc1.tar.bz2
```

It should spew a whole mess of output... but it should also create a new directory.  Go into that directory:

```
$ cd alsa-utils-1.0.15rc1
```

Now, start compiling:

```
$ ./configure && make && sudo make install
```

You can just copy and paste that.  The first script ("./configure", that usually comes with most source packages) will attempt to detect most of your computer's settings in order to tailor the package for your machine.  The second command (make), will begin compiling the package based on the first script's findings.  The third command (sudo make install) will give you root privileges (sudo) for just this command (make) because it needs to insert the compiled files (install) into your system directories.

If you got no errors, then all went well!  If you did get errors, let me know and maybe I can help with that.  You might run into "Sufficiently new version of libasound not found.", or something like that.  You could probably get around this with the following command:

```
$ sudo aptitude install libasound2-dev
```

That should install the newest version into your system.  If that still doesn't work, then just continue anyway, and come back to it after you've compiled the next file (I'm thinking on my toes, but the next package might actually contain the version of libasound that this thing needs, and if so, I'm sorry for telling you to do this one first!).

Now, backup one directory:

```
$ cd ..
```

You should be back in the directory with the three tar.bz2 files.  Now, extract the alsa-libs file:

```
$ tar jxvf alsa-lib-1.0.15rc3.tar.bz2
```

Then go into that directory:

```
$ cd alsa-lib-1.0.15rc3
```

Then compile and install:

```
$ ./configure && make && sudo make install
```

If this one worked, and the previous one (alsa-utils) didn't, then go back into that directory now and try compiling/installing it again.

After you're done with that, back out of that directory:

```
$ cd ..
```

Now extract the last file (alsa-driver):

```
$ tar jxvf alsa-driver-1.0.15rc3.tar.bz2
```

Go into that directory:

```
$ cd alsa-driver-1.0.15rc3.tar.bz2
```

Compile and install it:

```
$ ./configure && make && sudo make install
```

Finally, run alsaconf:

```
$ sudo alsaconf
```

That should bring you to a screen within the terminal that will attempt to begin looking for your sound card (hopefully, of course, finding it).  Select the "Intel" sound card from the list (if it shows you one), and that should be it!

You may need to restart your computer, but after this, the sound should be working fine!

Also keep in mind that you will probably not need to compile these things yourself as soon as the final release of alsa comes out in November (there may be a package available specifically for Ubuntu 7.10 once the final release is out).

Let me know if this helped you!  I may include a tutorial for this on my website in the future!

--JoeLinux

[Learn About Linux - A World Without Windows]("http://www.learnaboutlinux.net")
```
http://www.learnaboutlinux.net
```

---

### Post by bpickel on 2007-10-26
I will be the last to tell you to do this but..... If you have been uninstalling reinstalling GStreamer and such you may be facing a reinstall. If you get to that point immediately after install follow the HD Intel guide posted above-

You may also try this :
Go to synaptic and install the pulseaudio-esound-compat
package, esound is the old sound daemon and Gutsy has now employed pulseaudio. I have seen posts about this working. We are all rooting for you. Once you have passed this hurdle you will be able to enjoy what you have begun

Thanks for hanging in there. I have walked away from Ubuntu once or twice in frustration but find myself back. I am an IS Manager at an accounting firm and with a little  work I have my machine in Ubuntu  90% of the time even at work.

And though I am sure may have done this go to System > Administration > Update Manager and make sure you are all up to date. May be some magic hiding in the update hat :-)

It loads quickly, FF is better after some of the suggestions I received here regarding config changes and the "adblock" is worth its weight in gold.  Video looks good...wish I had sound to go along with it.  The repository is designed well and functional.  Apps through the repository are easy to install and uninstall. 

Just like I said earlier.  If someone is serious about using Linux, the wise thing to do is research the hardware that is well supported before installing.  It saves a lot of time and frustration and gives you the best possible Linux experience.  Being someone knowledgeable about computers, if you jump into something with unsupported software, it's kind of your own fault if you have all of this trouble.

I think the real bet is supposed to be on software, not hardware support.

---

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-10-26
> **professor fate said:**
> Generally speaking, I'm of the opinion that to confront and deal with some of these driver issues one has to be a bit of an uber geek.  I don't mind...I'm likely one too, but I can make a quick mental count of how many people I know that would say, "F this...I'm going back to my other computer".
Yep. This happens all the time, actually, and I don't blame anyone for not wanting to deal with the hardware issues that some boxes will cause for Linux. 

One of Canonical's stated goals is to combat Windows' current status as the default operating system for desktop users. I don't think any of the project leaders are under the illusion that it's ready to do this yet, but it is making progress. The deals with Dell and System76 have made it available preinstalled on hardware that *will* work, much like the way Windows systems work. Aside from that, I think the developers have been doing a good job of improving the ease of installation with every release. 

So, no, it's not "there" yet. It does work extremely well on a lot of computers, though, and the amount of progress made just this calendar year is a good sign.

### Post by jasay on 2007-10-26
> **professor fate said:**
> Joe, I followed every step.  I did get the error message you said I might get, but used the other script to fix it.  In any case, all went well beside that, but the very last step sudo alsaconf comes back as an unknown command.  I restarted the computer and tried the command again, but no luck.  I still get the same messages about GStreamer and no sound control when selecting the volume icon on desktop.

I'm neither an expert on compiling or sound in linux, but I'll throw this out there anyway.  

Alsaconf is not included in Ubuntu but is included in the version of alsa-utils you downloaded.  Since alsaconf could not be found it was likely not compiled/installed correctly and so you may be missing other tools from that package as well.  

In fact, I just tried compiling alsa-utils on a fresh version of Gutsy and ran into missing dependencies.  After running this command```
sudo apt-get build-dep alsa-utils
``` it compiled correctly.  I didn't run "make install" to see if it installed correctly because I don't want to mess with my system.  I did find the compiled binary in the alsa-utils-1.0.15rc1/alsaconf folder and it seemed to run correctly.

In case you were wondering, Ubuntu includes asoundconf instead of asaconf and it works something like this (I think, like I said, I'm no expert):
```
asoundconf list #will show a list of detected cards (yours is probably something like ICH8)
asoundconf set-default-card XXXX #where XXXX is the card listed above, this should set said card as default
```

Edit:

Forgot to mention.  You might have similar problem with the other two packages so a "sudo apt-get build-dep . . ." may be needed there too.  Also, I like running ./configure, make, and sudo make install separately because it's easier to watch for errors (which are generally shown at the end of each individual comman).

### Post by JoeLinux117 on 2007-10-26
I just compiled alsa-utils, and I checked to make sure that it creates "alsaconf".  It sure does, it's in there.

Not sure why it didn't "install" for you, Prof Fate.  Although just to make sure, within the same directory that you compiled alsa-utils (which should be the alsa-utils-1.0.15rc1 directory), there should be a directory called "alsaconf" (probably only after you've compiled it properly).  Within that directory, is a file called "alsaconf".  If just running "alsaconf" didn't work (maybe it didn't install to /usr/bin or /usr/local/bin, or maybe it's not located in your PATH variable), then you can try this from within the "alsa-utils-1.0.15rc1/alsaconf" directory:

```
$ chmod a+x alsaconf
```
```
$ sudo ./alsaconf
```

That should run the "alsaconf" command for you, and you should see that screen that I was talking about earlier that will attempt to detect your sound card.

I know what you mean about having to be sort of a geek to get some things working, but remember that this is a driver issue, and it's really up to the 3rd party vendors to make this happen.

Look at it this way (I'm a huge football fan so please bear with me):  You've got one star wide receiver, that everyone knows well (Windows).  You've got this newcomer (Linux), who might be even better than the other receiver, but no one will ever know him, and his career will never progress, if the quarterback (mainstream corporations) never throws him the ball.

I think the ideal behind Linux is fantastic, and the functionality is even better.  I just wish that companies would be more willing to explore other options than Windows in order to help computing in general advance more, for everyone.

*I hope I didn't make anyone cry, lol*  :)

And if you are concerned that you have to be somewhat of a geek to get through some issues, remember, that's why we are here!!!  Just find us on the forums!  lol

Again, I hope this helped!

--JoeLinux

[Learn About Linux - A World Without Windows]("http://www.learnaboutlinux.net")

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-10-26
Every time I've gotten the "C compiler cannot create executables" message it was because I forgot to install the build tools. If you haven't already, type this into a terminal:
```
sudo apt-get install build-essential
```

### Post by FuturePilot on 2007-10-26
If you do happen to try to get the sound working try using this patch
[https://bugtrack.alsa-project.org/alsa-bug/file_download.php?file_id=2057&type=bug]("https://bugtrack.alsa-project.org/alsa-bug/file_download.php?file_id=2057&type=bug")
After you extract the alsa-driver archive, unpack this archive and copy the 2 files into alsa-driver-1.0.xx/pci/hda/
And then run 
```
./configure --with-cards=hda-intel
make
sudo makeinstall
```
And add 
```
options snd-hda-intel model=toshiba
```
to the end of /etc/modprobe.d/alsa-base
```
gksudo gedit /etc/modprobe.d/alsa-base
```
Worth a shot

---

### Post by Can+~ on 2007-10-26
> **professor fate said:**

you're using nautilus right? (the default "explorer")? If true, then you need root power, do the following.. although is not "secure":

Alt+F2, type:
> gksudo nautilus

If you're using terminal for the operation, then it's easier, use sudo before anything to have root power:
> sudo <command>

If this is not what you want, then I misunderstood your question :confused:

### Post by dragonfyre13 on 2007-10-26
OK, let's take a step back.

Go through joe's instructions. Instead of stringing the commands together with && (That executes the commands one right after another) split them up.

```
sudo -s
./configure
make
make install
```

Now, execute each of the above commands, and then paste the output into the forum. We should be able to figure out the problem quickly and easily.

It's a simple driver compile, people have been doing it since before I was born (I'm a young pup) and I don't think that there is anything radically different in this one that we can't solve. Your friend still has more than enough time to win this thing, and while this isn't the most ideal scenario (Not the best first impression) it is, believe it or not, one of the best to start out with in my mind.

Even computer geeks like us need to know the OS itself before we can really be comfortable in any environment. I built custom restore disks for windows prior to switching full time to Ubuntu. I was comfortable there. Now when I run into an issue, I drop to the commandline and immediately type ps -ef, which of course frustrates me to no end when I find out there isn't a ps command in windows. ^_^

It's a matter of comfortability, and after this ordeal, you will feel like a linux king. "New, unsupported, bleeding edge hardware? I've solved it!"

---

### Post by Can+~ on 2007-10-26
> **dragonfyre13 said:**
> OK, let's take a step back.

Go through joe's instructions. Instead of stringing the commands together with && (That executes the commands one right after another) split them up.

```
sudo -s
./configure
make
make install
```

Now, execute each of the above commands, and then paste the output into the forum. We should be able to figure out the problem quickly and easily.

It's a simple driver compile, people have been doing it since before I was born (I'm a young pup) and I don't think that there is anything radically different in this one that we can't solve. Your friend still has more than enough time to win this thing, and while this isn't the most ideal scenario (Not the best first impression) it is, believe it or not, one of the best to start out with in my mind.

Even computer geeks like us need to know the OS itself before we can really be comfortable in any environment. I built custom restore disks for windows prior to switching full time to Ubuntu. I was comfortable there. Now when I run into an issue, I drop to the commandline and immediately type ps -ef, which of course frustrates me to no end when I find out there isn't a ps command in windows. ^_^

It's a matter of comfortability, and after this ordeal, you will feel like a linux king. "New, unsupported, bleeding edge hardware? I've solved it!"

Yeah, it first starts like "copy+paste" commands, then you say "what does 'sudo' means" and start learning, some always useful commands and explanations:

**sudo / gksudo** : In linux there is you (as a user) and there is also the root account, which is the all mighty administrator. You can get some of its power with sudo for a little while, or gksudo for a graphical input (that blackout effect).

**ls** : ls is the short for "list", it lists the files in a folder, much like "dir" in windows (dir is also available). Some modifiers are -a (all), -l (lowercase "L", to list things detailed).

**cd** : cd means to enter into a folder. If you want to go back "cd .." is the way.

**cp, mv** : copy and move, both work pretty much the same way: "mv fileA directory/fileB". You can also use this to rename files or make backup copies. Nobody likes this commands since everyone got used to "ctrl+C, ctrl+V" xD.

**rm** : delete something. ("remove")

**man** : man is the short for manual, if you want to know about a particular command, use "man <command>". Example: "man rm". To quit man, just press "q".

**make, make install** : this commands are.. well some way of easy compiling things. See.. compiling is the process of converting a source file into a working binary application, you need a compiler which reads the source and translates it into "machine code". when you use make, you're just saying to execute a certain file that compiles for you, and installs it (in case of make install).

### Post by st4rdr1ft3r on 2007-10-27
I've put them in my apt repo. Just add:
```
deb http://ubuntu.digitalnetworks.co.uk gutsy main
```
to your /etc/apt/sources.list

Then run
```
wget http://ubuntu.digitalnetworks.co.uk/key.gpg -O- | sudo apt-key add -
sudo apt-get update
sudo apt-get upgrade
```

And apt will spot the newer versions and upgrade them for you.

---

### Post by st4rdr1ft3r on 2007-10-27
one thing that it might be is that the laptop speakers might be attached to the sound card through digital, which you have to check a switch. it's in the sound control panel but you have to edit the preferences to display it.

right click the volume control and select open volume control.

go to edit>preferences and enable all of them. to enable digital it's in the switches tab, IEC958, make sure headphone is enabled as well, then try turning all the sliders up full .

### Post by aaaantoine on 2007-10-27
I ran into very similar problems with building ALSA over two years ago.  In fact, here's the thread:

[http://www.linuxquestions.org/questions/linux-software-2/alsa-utils-1.0.8-fails-during-make-294058/](http://www.linuxquestions.org/questions/linux-software-2/alsa-utils-1.0.8-fails-during-make-294058/)

One user posts, "On Ubuntu Feisty Fawn, I've installed "gettext" and "ja-trans" packages, then this problem has solved !"  This should solve the problem relating to "t-ja.gmo".

Also, to reduce the number of potential problems when trying make, first type make clean.

### Post by Frak on 2007-10-27
> **Vadi said:**
> Well of course, but that's simply a human element, no? I doubt they're purposely ignoring bug reports.
They do it for three reasons

1. If it isn't important (hardware compatability isn't the case)
2. If the driver for Linux does not exist (Also is not the case)
3. If it is a duplicate bug (most likely the case)

### Post by aaaantoine on 2007-10-27
The fact that you have sound out of the headphone port is a great sign.  To get it to work out of your speakers, you may only have to fiddle with the volume manager.  For instance, my speaker volume comes in through the "Surround" sound channel.  Weird, huh?

So try out some of the different sound channels and see if you can find your sound.

---

### Post by dasunst3r on 2007-10-27
To get the internal speakers working, try this:
1. Go to the volume control
2. Go to Edit, then Preferences
3. Make sure that all the tracks are visible, then click Close.
4. Try looking for a checkbox that says "External Amplifier."  Make sure that's checked.

I remember having to do this back when I had my Dell Inspiron 6000.

---

### Post by professor fate on 2007-10-27
[SIZE="6"]Drop your linen and start your grinin'. I have full sound![/SIZE]

[SIZE="5"]Four installs and one kernel update later![/SIZE]

I found the answer here.  It was posted three days ago.

add the following to /etc/modprobe.d/alsa-base: options snd-hda-intel model=5stack

---

### Post by WanderingKnight on 2007-10-28
> 
Wow, it's rather mind blowing to see how many hits this thread has received. I really didn't notice it until this morning, and God knows I didn't expect it. Amazing interest and support at this site. Thanks a lot. 

Well, I hope it's an incentive for you to explore a bit more the world of Linux. I know this perhaps was a rather traumatic first experience, but nowadays it's becoming less and less common. With supported hardware (which is not obscure or expensive at all) any Linux distro worth its salt is an awesome deal. What I hope is that you don't forget about it once you've gotten back to Vista. By far, the best thing about Linux (besides the fact of it being free as in beer and as in speech) is the community, and the sense of *being together with someone*, of feeling peer trust and support, the sense of *belonging* to something, of being part of something, instead of relying solely on a faceless corporation who just wants your money. You have just discovered the best thing about Linux, and I hope it leaves a positive impression on you.

I'm not going to yell at you if you turn to Vista and never look back again, because you have made your choice, you have evaluated the options accordingly and made your decision. I respect you a lot for the effort you put into this, being just a newbie Linux user. But please keep in mind that such a support on the Windows side would've been rare, to say the least. Of course, it's up to you to value what you prefer for your PC.

Congratulations on getting the sound working, and I honestly hope you stick around with Ubuntu even after the bet is fulfilled :)

### Post by professor fate on 2007-10-28
So far all of the Mozilla plug-ins for 64bit Gutsy have worked for me.  I was truly surprised so see that the HP drivers worked.  In any case, some comments and thoughts on Ubuntu.  Also, keep in mind I'm knee deep into Windows.  For example, I have a developer edition of Vista, so I have every flavor in all supported languages...and it's legal  LOL.   

Pluses for Ubuntu:
1. The repository setup and add/remove software are the bomb. It makes it very easy to select from a variety of software.
2. No anti-virus or spyware updates to contend with
3. Fast as snot on a waxed surfboard. This laptop is quick.
4. Bootup and wireless connection is very fast. I normally don't turn my computers off, but those are desktops.  Laptops are different in that respect.
5. Compiz is very nice.  I'm not into the cube stuff, but there are other nice features.  I don't know which one this is, but one I'm looking for I've seen on Youtube.  If one has many windows or folders open, they hit a button and then all the windows/folders get reduced in size and are spread out on the desktop.  

The downside:
The Crysis demo was released on the 26th and I can't play it. :(
I haven't looked into it, but does Ubuntu have a DX10 emulation for video drivers?  Crysis on a DX10 card is ungodly.  Don't know how many gamers we have here.

---

### Post by st4rdr1ft3r on 2007-10-28
> **professor fate said:**
> I don't know which one this is, but one I'm looking for I've seen on Youtube.  If one has many windows or folders open, they hit a button and then all the windows/folders get reduced in size and are spread out on the desktop.

It's the scale plugin. You can set it up so it happens when you move the mouse to the bottom left corner of the screen in the compiz config settings manager.

---

### Post by Forlong on 2007-10-28
> **professor fate said:**
> 5. Compiz is very nice.  I'm not into the cube stuff, but there are other nice features.  I don't know which one this is, but one I'm looking for I've seen on Youtube.  If one has many windows or folders open, they hit a button and then all the windows/folders get reduced in size and are spread out on the desktop.
That's called Scale. Just install **compizconfig-settings-manager** and set it the way you like (see [here](http://forlong.blogage.de/article/2007/8/29/How-to-set-up-Compiz-Fusion) for reference).

---

### Post by WanderingKnight on 2007-10-28

### Post by kevdog on 2007-10-28
Dont be to quick to put down gdesklets.  I have one laptop that has a rage ATI 128mb mobility graphics card -- it cant run sophisticated composting managers so compiz fusion is a no go.  gdesklets has a much less sophisticated docking program, however it does the job for older computers.  I know it seems strange, but no everyone can run compiz-fusion.

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-10-28
> **professor fate said:**
> Hi everyone,

Thanks for all the great suggestions.  I will certainly try them out.  On a side note, I tossed my SD card in the front slot of the laptop to look at some pictures.  HOLY BAJESUS...I cannot believe how fast Ubuntu renders them.  I have several hundred pics, all 1mb or larger, and it did them in less than 10 seconds.  That impressed the hell out of me.
That's more likely the effect of the 64-bit OS rather than Linux vs. Windows. For a lot of tasks, the cpu width makes little difference, but for anything like rendering, encoding or compiling ... that's when you see the boost. But, yeah, Ubuntu is usually a bit more responsive than Windows, except with Firefox. :(

Anyway, if I can add one more suggestion: give KDE a try. You can install it alongside the default Gnome desktop by typing this in the terminal:
```
sudo apt-get install kubuntu-desktop
```
It'll ask you to choose between "gdm" and "kdm" after doing this. Just leave it in gdm. Now you can use the "options" menu in the login screen to select a KDE session. It's not to everyone's taste, but some of us prefer it.

---

### Post by r3m0t on 2007-10-28
I just tried installing edubuntu-desktop from aptitude, and it doesn't want to uninstall the ubuntu-desktop package.

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-10-28
> **lyndaj70 said:**
> Hey, can you do that with edubuntu without it wiping out your gnome desktop?  I would like to install it for my kid to use but when I tried to go through synaptic it wanted to uninstall my gnome-desktop, which I'm happy with.  Any hints?:confused:
What exactly did it tell you? Both kubuntu-desktop and ubuntu-desktop are "dummy" packages, meaning that uninstalling them doesn't actually do anything. If you run the command I gave you, it will warn you before it tries to remove anything, but it shouldn't need to.

---

### Post by American_Outcast on 2007-10-28
> **p_quarles said:**
> What exactly did it tell you? Both kubuntu-desktop and ubuntu-desktop are "dummy" packages, meaning that uninstalling them doesn't actually do anything. If you run the command I gave you, it will warn you before it tries to remove anything, but it shouldn't need to.


So they are dummy packages. What do they install just the packages that normally come with an Ubuntu flavor and the display managers?

---

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-10-28
> **American_Outcast said:**
> So they are dummy packages. What do they install just the packages that normally come with an Ubuntu flavor and the display managers?
That's my understanding. Basically, the *ubuntu-desktop packages are lists of dependencies, so installing them will automatically fetch all of the correct packages. But they don't actually contain anything in and of themselves. 

If I'm wrong, someone please correct me.

### Post by Can+~ on 2007-10-28
I installed kubuntu-desktop on a ubuntu-desktop back in Feisty, and as I remember:

-The boot and login screen was all KDE. But I could login into Gnome or KDE from there.
-Although I liked KDE, I didn't like to have a mixture of both icons/files/folders in one and another, completely messy.
-When I entered in Gnome, I noticed it had KDE icons all over the place.

Conclusion:
Some people may not worry about this issues, but me, personally, hate to have an hybrid in which elements are mixed up. I would love if I could login in gnome, and have a whole gnome-ish config, and a separate whole KDE-ish config. Maybe as separate partitions.. but nah.

KDE was nice though, I'm waiting for KDE 4, and maybe I'll check it out again.

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-10-28
If you got a KDE login screen, that's because you selected "kdm" when the post-installation dialogue came up. If you'd selected "gdm", you wouldn't have had any of those issues.

### Post by Heresy on 2007-10-29
> **professor fate said:**
> Well, if I can't get this sound to work it's a sure bet I've won.  LOL.  I've spent the better part of 6 hours and have tried every single link on this forum trying to get this sound card to work.  Honestly, the last time I had this much trouble with an OS was with Windows 95.

Honestly it was a major problem with my Dell laptop and sound.  
Its something about Dell and Ubuntu. 
I had to fight the drives because they were seen as scsi devices, modprobing never got the sound detected. 
I did get it to work one weird way.  

**I did it on 2 different laptops and one would not boot** 
 
I renamed the xorg.conf 
sudo rm /etc/X11/xorg.conf /etc/X11/xorg.back
Then did a startx.
It flashed a bit.  I rebooted and everything came back up. 

I am passing this along solely as a "If you really want to try item"

### Post by professor fate on 2007-10-29
It's midnight and I REALLY need to get some sleep.  The problem is there's so damn much stuff one can do with this OS.  Tonight I wanted to install HandBrake for ripping DVDs. ("whisper"....I use this on Vista because it's great).  So, it only seemed natural to use it on Ubuntu.("whisper"...I also use VLC on Vista too).  Well, I wasn't getting any response out of HandBreak.  So then I thought to try out the DVD playback.  Well wouldn't you know there have been quite a few posts regarding no DVD playback and libdvdcss2.  Sure, not a problem.  Just find it and install it.  BUT...what if you're using 64bit like I am?  Each time I tried to install it I got a "wrong architecture" message.  This sent me on a trip around the world via the Inet looking for a suitably fixed libdvdcss2 that'll work with 64bit.  I found it, but it wasn't easy.  

OK...time for sleep.  For those that haven't used HandBrake, I strongly recommend it.  

PF

### Post by cellerit on 2007-10-29
EDIT: Woops you got it working already forget it.

> **professor fate said:**
> Well, if I can't get this sound to work it's a sure bet I've won.  LOL.  I've spent the better part of 6 hours and have tried every single link on this forum trying to get this sound card to work.  Honestly, the last time I had this much trouble with an OS was with Windows 95.

From here: [http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=577469](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=577469)
This one worked for me.

------
Perform audio fix (I've included my steps, and alternatives from others - choose only one)

    * Here's what I did... (* see below for other models this is reported to have worked on)
          o sudo apt-get install libc6-dev
          o sudo apt-get install patch
          o wget ftp:// ftp.alsa-project.org/pub/driver/alsa-driver-1.0.15.tar.bz2 (remove the space after the double-slash)
          o tar xvpjf alsa-driver-1.0.15.tar.bz2
          o cd alsa-driver-1.0.15
          o ./configure --with-cards=hda-intel,emu10k1
          o make
          o sudo make install
----

Anyhow, you may have had better luck with Ubuntu had you tried it with a pre-installed laptop (after all, that's how most everyone gets Windows).  The manufacturer makes sure that an OS on a new computer is compatible with ALL of the hardware (so after years of only selling Windows computers, the need for the Windows HCL has pretty much gone away).

By installing Ubuntu on a computer pre-built with Windows, it's like trying out a V8 engine on a Ford Escort.  It just wasn't originally meant for it.

The more that manufacturers such as Dell begin selling new computers pre-installed with Linux on them, the more that hardware will have drivers for Linux from the get-go.  I'm so glad that you were that willing to try, and I commend you wholeheartedly.  Take care!

--JoeLinux

### Post by professor fate on 2007-10-29
Hello,

My goal for the day was to get HandBrakeGTK installed.  That's the GUI version of HandBrake for Linux.  For those interested in seeing what HandBrake can do, I've posted a video up on [http://thepiratebay.com](http://thepiratebay.com).  It's the Bourne Supremacy.  If you run a search for it under Movies, you'll see my username next to it.  Of course this is strictly for “educational” purposes to see what this app can do. Afterward you are encouraged to delete it. (Covering my ***). :)

My next task is to get KEX 1190 to play on the laptop.  I listen to it on my desktop every evening at home, so it's a must have.  HandBrake was one as well.  I can tick that off the list.  

I also want to add that the help guides here are excellent.  

On another note, I've been reading some of the other threads.  One topic that caught my interest was the “readiness” threads.  Is Ubuntu ready for the masses? question.  I think Ubuntu is ready for whoever wants to use it.  As far as I can tell at this point, its only shortcoming is driver support, and I suspect specialized enterprise software.  I only say this because of my years in various business industries, we have never used, to the best of my recollection, any open source software or Linux platforms.  I'm sure there are huge companies using Linux for data warehousing.  This is just one small example of what I mean though.  [http://www.businessobjects.com/](http://www.businessobjects.com/) This is the kind of company you don't hear much about, but they do millions and millions of dollars in custom software application design and support.  It just happens to be for the MS platform.

Is Ubuntu stable?  Yeah, I think it is.  Can it take on MS in the home computing environment?  Sure, I don't see why not.  Can it take on MS in a business environment?  Of course, if the software is available.

### Post by Frak on 2007-10-29

> This is all fine and dandy for ripping and burning dvds but does any one know how to mount them to use without a dvd player with a iso or whatever the dvd version of an iso is.

```

### Post by xat_ on 2007-10-30
Hello; briefly skimmed through this thread so my response will be as brief. Foremost, commendable effort on your part. You are not exactly within the subset I'd classify as the "average user." Such a person would've ditched Ubuntu very quickly. What many people have warned at the beginning has proven true; there's a lot you can do as far as general tasks go, but when something doesn't JustWork (as the mantra goes), it's possible to hit massive blocks that require serious work. Hats off for going beyond the average user and deciding to take on the task of learning.

As far as Crysis goes, I'm not sure you'll be able to run it in Wine let alone Vista - the system requirements are steep and the demo seems to be an earlier build which performs a bit poorly even on lower settings. That said, you're welcome to try. Shader support in Wine is somewhat limited, but that doesn't mean you're totally without options. I've been enjoying gaming on Linux (Orange Box works decently); maybe the Crysis demo will work somewhat. No guarantees though.
Minor details if you're just glossing over this:
-Shader support in development
-No FSAA

You can check Wine's DirectX development to-do here:
[http://wiki.winehq.org/DirectX-ToDo](http://wiki.winehq.org/DirectX-ToDo)

If you are going to be using Wine it may also be in your interest to add a Wine repo that keeps up with Wine's release cycle (a new version every two weeks). More information here:
[http://winehq.org/site/download-deb](http://winehq.org/site/download-deb)

As for sound, it sounds like (pun not intended) it really was a matter of new hardware / no drivers (and possibly an architecture mixup when you first downloaded Gutsy; should've asked for your system specs foremost, perhaps). Sadly, with all of the hardware out and coming, there's no way to best speedy support from the hardware manufacturers. And we know where they're aligned. You've come in at a lucky time though. Had this been just a few years ago, the bet would've been decided at your first install.

---

### Post by TitanKing on 2007-10-30
Hi prof, interesting threat, I like the idea. However, if I may point out a few things.

I use Ubuntu in my profession, work I do on it cant be done in Windows. Its very much like comparing apples with bananas. The Linux road is not easy, takes allot of guts and sweat with some swearing, the reward at the end is great. After really learning Linux you will realize the power at your fingertips. It is a great production tool.

In the odd events where I need to use/test a Windows application I would use Virtualbox as it integrates seemlesly. I really think you should not compare the two, rather concentrate on the real power of Linux, the few minor detail Windows can do that Linux can't will be replaced with real important subjects that Linux is capable of that Windows is years from.

Windows is created for the end user, Linux is for from the end user to the hard core computer user. My wife also recently moved over to Linux, and up to now, there was nothing she could do on Windows that she could not do on Linux, it is completely possible.

Regards,
Titan

### Post by dasunst3r on 2007-10-30
To get KEX 1190 (or nearly any Windows Media stream in general) working, here's what you might want to do (depending on what everyone else says):[LIST=1]
[*]Add the [Medibuntu]("http://www.medibuntu.org/") repository
[*]Install these packages: mplayer, mozilla-mplayer, and w32codecs
[*]Restart Firefox[/LIST]

### Post by Can+~ on 2007-10-30
> **dasunst3r said:**
> For those of you who have experience with both 32-bit and 64-bit on the same machine, was there any performance jump when you made the switch?  I'm thinking about doing the same after the semester ends.

Intel CPU's with a 64 bit architecture are the ones that have a real impact; AMD hybrids can stand both pretty well. Anyhow, everyone should be upgrading to 64 bit if possible, since this is the next step on hardware/software development.

---

### Post by Inxsible on 2007-10-30
> **dasunst3r said:**
> For those of you who have experience with both 32-bit and 64-bit on the same machine, was there any performance jump when you made the switch?  I'm thinking about doing the same after the semester ends.I had Feisty 32 on my machine and went to Gutsy 64 now.

You won't see a performance jump in smaller apps. But if you perform processor intensive tasks, then you may be able to see 30-40 % increase in speed. Tasks like audio/video encoding, ripping burning etc.

Even if I am not performing those kinda tasks, just knowing that I am taking full advantage of my hardware kinda satisfies me :D

Oh! and if you are afraid something might break, this may give you some hope.
I installed Gutsy 64 bit and everything worked out of the box !
in fact my suspend and hibernate also work (they didnt in Feisty), although I am not sure if that's because of Gutsy or because of 64 bit

### Post by professor fate on 2007-10-30
> **Inxsible said:**
> I had Feisty 32 on my machine and went to Gutsy 64 now.

You won't see a performance jump in smaller apps. But if you perform processor intensive tasks, then you may be able to see 30-40 % increase in speed. Tasks like audio/video encoding, ripping burning etc.

Even if I am not performing those kinda tasks, just knowing that I am taking full advantage of my hardware kinda satisfies me :D

Oh! and if you are afraid something might break, this may give you some hope.
I installed Gutsy 64 bit and everything worked out of the box !
in fact my suspend and hibernate also work (they didnt in Feisty), although I am not sure if that's because of Gutsy or because of 64 bit

I will add to this that in some instances certain things have been more difficult to come by.  For instance, the libdvdcss2 file for 64bit.  I tried installing the one that came on the 64bit CD that I burned, but it said "wrong architexture".  LOL.  I found the right one but it did take some time.  I don't know what others may share the same difficulty since I've on had to look for one.  64bit is nice though.

### Post by sonicsmooth on 2007-10-30
If you're a windows user I think a better bet (for your behalf) would have been against XP.  Vista is still full of bugs, slowness, over-obnoxious security, bad drivers, etc.

XP has been filtered through the past several years, is a complete breeze to install, has all the drivers, and basically is *extremely* quick and easy to get set up.  Sure you have to pay for it, but how much is your sanity worth.

For all the rabid anti-MS religiosity being spewed by coffee dude, Microsoft spends MILLIONS of dollars on usability, usability, usability, and focusing on the user.  No, they don't always get it right, and many things are quite frankly retarded, but to say they deliberately screw the user is like saying the church deliberately sends people to hell.  They may be mistaken in their efforts, but in its unceasing effort to make money, their goal is to reach the most number of users possible with the most amount of hardware, which unfortunatly means dumbing it down to the nth degree.  With windows, as with other things, you have to know what you're doing, but in different ways.  With Windows, for instance, you can be security-smart by NOT using IE or OE.  IE7 is better, but compared to other browsers I think it lacks speed and grace and convenience compared to Opera, and yes the venerated, sanctified, Holy Firefox.

I have an MSEE, am not a computer beginner, work in a very technical field, have been playing with linux on and off for 10 years (mostly off), and can follow the howtos to a letter.  On a few occasions I've had mini home servers for various reasons in the past, but this time around with Ubuntu I'm having a HELL of a time getting the most basic stuff working: dvd (maybe a hardware problem), sound, NFS, SMB, static IP address, xine/video, HDTV tuner.  It took a few weeks to get X to work at the correct resolution on my HDTV (I wound up having to disable a max-clock-frequency-check or something in the driver). I had to disable wacom for some completely random and stupid reason in X, I think having to do with video playback (why was it even in there?  I don't have a wacom board), the touchpad on my laptop is too sensitive and I can't understand how to update/install the driver, compiling and installing stuff works about 50-60% of the time.  I've had to randomly run ldconfig a few times to get new drivers to work.  In a word, yes Ubuntu installs easily the first time for completely *basic* usage, including wireless, and the driver support is surprisingly great, but if you want to change *anything*, it NEVER EVER EVER works the first time.  Likewise when running mplayer or xine you will be asked to specify things like video driver, decoder, demuxer, file formats, etc, etc.  Any truly user-oriented app would NOT require you to choose this stuff, although of course the option should be there if you want to change it.  Windows is totally easy to get set up and only pisses you off in ways you're used to.

Anyway, good luck with your bet.  Try setting up a home media server with NFS, hardware-accelerated video playback, 1920x1080 resolution on DVI output, Bluetooth connectivity, HDTV tuner, and MythTv (which includes MySQL)  vs. Vista Media Center and tell me how you do.  You will probably fail before you pull your hair out.

Michael

---

### Post by Crashmaxx on 2007-10-30
> **sonicsmooth said:**
> Try setting up a home media server with NFS, hardware-accelerated video playback, 1920x1080 resolution on DVI output, Bluetooth connectivity, HDTV tuner, and MythTv (which includes MySQL)  vs. Vista Media Center and tell me how you do.  You will probably fail before you pull your hair out.


Let's see, I have the majority of that on this machine right now. I used NFS on it, and it was a LOT easier then Samba was, hardware-accelerated playback works fine, I'm sure 1920x1080 would work if I had a bigger monitor, otherwise I'm using 1440x900 with DVI with no issues, never used Bluetooth though, I prefer my dual SDTV tuner, but I haven't seen HDTV ones being any trouble as long as you buy a supported one, and both the MythTV backend and frontend are running great.

All on this machine, while downloading torrents, browsing the web, and whatever else I need to do. With close to zero downtime and using a RAID 1 array to make sure that it stays that way even if a drive dies. And this machine won't even run Vista if I wanted. Apparently its "too slow", though it's plenty fast for me.

I've had very few problems for such a massively complex setup. I even got Compiz-Fusion running without needing to reboot. Windows systems can be pretty pathetic in comparison. I've been system admin of Windows Server 2003 machines before, and they can't even stay up for a week. They have to reboot at least once every update. And sometimes they like to just have services die with no warning or reason. Or drop a bunch of emails to oblivion. Maintaining those will make you pull your hair out.

Sorry to get off-topic again. Just thought I'd counter. Sure a preinstalled Vista MCE is easy, but massively restricted in what it can do. I'd go for a TiVo again before I went for that. Personally, I outgrew TiVo and by carefully selecting components had no trouble setting up a superior MythTV box.

### Post by bpickel on 2007-10-30
Are you using Firefox ? Or Swiftweasle. It might sound odd but you may need a script for greasemonkey that was written to  make foxnews video work. It also affects lots of other streaming media. The script is here :

[http://userscripts.org/scripts/show/1371](http://userscripts.org/scripts/show/1371)


You will need to be using FF (I believe). And install the greasemonkey add-on. There are install links in the page above that will install the script as long as you have the add-on installed and active. Let me know if that helps.

### Post by Crashmaxx on 2007-10-30
Might want to try Media Player Connectivity. It lets you open any stream with any player you want. Using it I had no trouble with KEX 1190, the annoying ad even played.
[https://addons.mozilla.org/en-US/firefox/addon/446](https://addons.mozilla.org/en-US/firefox/addon/446)

Once you find the stream URL you can do a couple things. Install streamtuner and play it with that, or even setup a simple script to open the URL with mplayer in the background. I do something similar to the later for XM online. Of course, they require a username and password, and they also supply current song info. The script logs in, opens mplayer, and displays the song info in a bubble in the corner. It was originally intended for MythTV, but I run it by itself a lot.

---

So far so good.  On occasion I do get a couple of error messages when listening to KEX 1190.  Something about not being able to get a connection, but it still plays.  I actually got a bit thrown off track with the conkyrc stuff.  It looks cool, so I've been playing around with it.  

The mouse pad on this laptop is really sensitive, so I've spent some time dealing with that.  FF still suffers from hickups, but nothing drastic; I'm getting the gray screen that other people have commented on.  

All said though, this is a nice OS the Ubuntu team has put together. The customization seems virtually endless.  Scale is the best thing since the mouse.  I use it all the time b/c I have so many windows open.  I've been tempted to buy a power cord for the Vista box, hook this one into the network and see how to get them to communicate with each other, but that would be an automatic lose on my part.  I can't turn that box on. I wouldn't be able to look my buddy in the face and say, "no..I never powered it up".  Besides, if I did get them to talk with each other, I couldn't tell him.  LOL.

### Post by macogw on 2007-11-01
> **professor fate said:**
> The mouse pad on this laptop is really sensitive, so I've spent some time dealing with that.  FF still suffers from hickups, but nothing drastic; I'm getting the gray screen that other people have commented on.  

The grey screen is Compiz Fusion's signal to you that the application is not responding.  Enlightenment does a similar thing: it makes the title bar flash glowing red.  You avoid staring at it waiting when the app has actually frozen, at least.

---

### Post by sloggerkhan on 2007-11-01
If an app grays out while your cpu is really loaded, there is a good chance it's not dead, just working.

### Post by xat_ on 2007-11-02
> **professor fate said:**
> What sort of performance hit would the computer take?  Does it feel laggy?

Your mileage may vary based on your specs, of course. VMs are definite memory hogs, and working simultaneously between the host and VM could induce some strain on the CPU (this scales well with multiple cores).

Little note/disclaimer about VirtualBox; their seamless integration feature is a bit cheap. The idea is essentially to mask everything but the windows themselves; no moving windows across workspaces, no accessing individual windows from your gnome task panel.

I have yet to try their newest version (came out as of Oct. 18th), however the version prior also had a known bug which yielded massive distortion of the background when no windows were floating (i.e. they're all minimized).

---

### Post by Exonimus on 2007-11-02
I stickied this topic because I'm really interested in how this continues. About virtualbox: I have it running on a 2ghz pc with 512mb ram, 192mb assigned to my virtual windows xp in Seamless mode. It's quite good, but not perfect yet. And it's a bit cheap because some other VM programs had it first, but you won't hear me complaining :P if you're interested, you can also do it another way.
[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=433359](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=433359)
but the seamless mode that comes with virtualbox is much less of a hassle. Actually, for me, it was no hassle at all. Just installing windows on a VM, installing the guest additions(2 mouse clicks) and pressing host key+l. done.

### Post by adam.tropics on 2007-11-03
> **Forlong said:**
> Flash is proprietary/closed source, so there's pretty much now way to provide an open source application for that.

I don't know if there's a commercial one, though (doubt it).

..But they are starting to listen I think. [Flex is in development for Linux]("http://labs.adobe.com/technologies/flex/flexbuilder_linux/"), and sounds pretty interesting, though on Linux it is plugin based (eclipse) only. Applications can be built as .swf and played in Flash Player.

I concede though, that this isn't flash as such, but I do like the direction. Funny thing on the [wikipedia]("http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adobe_Flex#Adobe_Flex_3_.28beta.29") site...users of version one were paying $15,000 per cpu...now they're giving us development releases for free....bet that won't last!!!

### Post by holiday on 2007-11-03
Your friend is either nuts - or loaded and feels sorry for your need for a laptop. Were you guys drunk?

Ubuntu can not do everything that Vista does - just like a sushi bar can't give you a Big Mac. Perhaps that's not fair - after all, Vista is not fast food. It's more like Jenny Craig. It gives you all you need in a glitzy package. All that's required of you is the patience to let it thaw. 

Here's my suggestion: if, after using Ubuntu for thirty days, you are willing to completely delete your Ubuntu partition, then you win. However, in that time you must demonstrate a reasonable facility with the Unix command line; an understanding of the subtleties of file permissions; explain the difference between Unix permissions and Windows permissions and how Windows permissions is a superior system... 

Some of the sweetest features of Unix don't even have a rudimentary version in Windows. 

If I was your friend, I'd bet a laptop that I can do things in Ubuntu that you have no chance in hell of coming even close to in Windows.

Who is this guy? He sounds interesting.

### Post by psyopper on 2007-11-04
Can you be more specific? Is the entire Firefox window grey, or is it just the browser portion of the window? Had Firefox worked properly sometime in the past? At what point did these symptoms arise?

This sounds vaguely like Nvidia black screen issue. There is a driver issue with Nvidia cards that allows the frame buffer to overload and render application windows black. Generally only affects (ir is it effects?) cards with less than 256 meg of dedicated memory.

What video chipset is on the laptop? Shared/dedicated video memory? What drivers are you using for it? I know you have done a couple of reinstalls but I am too lazy to go back through 50 pages of posts to figure this out - what is your current install, ie: Gutsy/Feisty, 32/64? Mind posting a copy of your /etc/X11/xorg.conf?

Having read this thread through as it developed I do realize some of these questions are sort of insulting given your experience level, but basic troubleshooting is the same regardless of what operating system you have installed....

---

### Post by Frak on 2007-11-04
This IS a bug, it has been connected to the relation between Firefox and Compiz, nothing with the graphics card.

[https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/compiz/+bug/124581](https://bugs.launchpad.net/ubuntu/+source/compiz/+bug/124581)

# xorg.conf (xorg X Window System server configuration file)
#
# This file was generated by dexconf, the Debian X Configuration tool, using
# values from the debconf database.
#
# Edit this file with caution, and see the xorg.conf manual page.
# (Type "man xorg.conf" at the shell prompt.)
#
# This file is automatically updated on xserver-xorg package upgrades *only*
# if it has not been modified since the last upgrade of the xserver-xorg
# package.
#
# If you have edited this file but would like it to be automatically updated
# again, run the following command:
#   sudo dpkg-reconfigure -phigh xserver-xorg

Section "Files"
EndSection

Section "InputDevice"
	Identifier	"Generic Keyboard"
	Driver		"kbd"
	Option		"CoreKeyboard"
	Option		"XkbRules"	"xorg"
	Option		"XkbModel"	"pc105"
	Option		"XkbLayout"	"us"
EndSection

Section "InputDevice"
	Identifier	"Configured Mouse"
	Driver		"mouse"
	Option		"CorePointer"
	Option		"Device"	"/dev/input/mice"
	Option		"Protocol"	"ImPS/2"
	Option		"ZAxisMapping"	"4 5"
	Option		"Emulate3Buttons"	"true"
EndSection

Section "InputDevice"
	Identifier	"Synaptics Touchpad"
	Driver		"synaptics"
	Option		"SendCoreEvents"	"true"
	Option		"Device"	"/dev/psaux"
	Option		"Protocol"	"auto-dev"
	Option		"HorizEdgeScroll"	"0"
EndSection

Section "InputDevice"
	Driver		"wacom"
	Identifier	"stylus"
	Option		"Device"	"/dev/input/wacom"
	Option		"Type"	"stylus"
	Option		"ForceDevice"	"ISDV4"# Tablet PC ONLY
EndSection

Section "InputDevice"
	Driver		"wacom"
	Identifier	"eraser"
	Option		"Device"	"/dev/input/wacom"
	Option		"Type"	"eraser"
	Option		"ForceDevice"	"ISDV4"# Tablet PC ONLY
EndSection

Section "InputDevice"
	Driver		"wacom"
	Identifier	"cursor"
	Option		"Device"	"/dev/input/wacom"
	Option		"Type"	"cursor"
	Option		"ForceDevice"	"ISDV4"# Tablet PC ONLY
EndSection

Section "Device"
	Identifier	"nVidia Corporation G80 [GeForce 8400M GS]"
	Driver		"nvidia"
	Busid		"PCI:1:0:0"
	Option		"AddARGBVisuals"	"True"
	Option		"AddARGBGLXVisuals"	"True"
	Option		"NoLogo"	"True"
EndSection

Section "Monitor"
	Identifier	"Generic Monitor"
	Option		"DPMS"
	Horizsync	28-64
	Vertrefresh	43-60
EndSection

Section "Screen"
	Identifier	"Default Screen"
	Device		"nVidia Corporation G80 [GeForce 8400M GS]"
	Monitor		"Generic Monitor"
	Defaultdepth	24
	SubSection "Display"
		Modes		"1280x1280"
	EndSubSection
EndSection

Section "ServerLayout"
	Identifier	"Default Layout"
  screen "Default Screen"
	Inputdevice	"Generic Keyboard"
	Inputdevice	"Configured Mouse"
	
	# Uncomment if you have a wacom tablet

	#	InputDevice     "eraser"	"SendCoreEvents"

### Post by Vadi on 2007-11-04
Compiz greys out a window when it's unresponsive, this has nothing to do with firefox itself.

Firefox does 'grey out' for me when loading java, that's because java loading causes firefox to be unresponsive 'till it loads! When it loads, it goes back to normal.

Just an idea, you may want to try Ubuntuzilla. What it does is actually replace your Ubuntu-installed version of Firefox with the official Mozilla release, which I have found to be a bit stable on my system (which is a dell Dimension E521 with 2GB of DDR2 and an AMD Dual-core processor running Gutsy). It keeps all of your settings and whatnot from your current firefox as well, so you may want to give it a try. Hope this hepls!!

Is the window going empty slate grey with nothing visible?

### Post by professor fate on 2007-11-05
> **Marc Hoffman said:**
> Hi Proffessor

How are you actually enjoying Ubuntu so far, despite the hiccups in setting your system up?

Hello,

It's 5:45am here and I'm about to hop on the exercise bike and watch a video I downloaded using Deluge Bit torrent.  I think overall Ubuntu, and I imagine this can probably be said for many Linux distros, is a very fine OS.  Undoubtedly it's number one strength is its ability to be customized to the n'th degree.  I've looked at many many posts here whereby users have submitted desktop screen shots of their systems and have been amazed by the variety.  

Oddly though, I think Ubuntu's greatest strength is also its greatest weakness, BUT...I should preface this by saying it depends on the type of user.  Let me put it this way.  Vista is like canned soup. You know exactly what you're going to get, and to a large degree the soup will be safe for consumption.  Ubuntu, or Linux in general, is like getting all of the ingredients for soup, but it's up to you to make it.  One can customize every little nuance to affect the taste, and because of this one can experience a flavor of the day if they will.  The former soup requires less imagination, but it's generally safe because it has been finely measured, processed, and canned.  The latter requires more imagination and possible risks for those that don't know the first thing about cooking, but has a potential of producing something extraordinary.  A good cook (e.g., someone computer savvy or at least an interest in it) is a great candidate for Linux.  A not so good cook (e.g., a "I just want it to work" person) is not a good candidate for Linux simply because they're more interested in....and this is the important part...eating, and not so much about what they're eating or how it's made.  

I'm in my early 40s. Most of my friends my age have little interest in tackling an OS.  My younger friends (e.g., the guy I have the bet with) are more computer savvy and like to tinker with this stuff. I've always been that way though too.  I was into computers in grade school, and have been hooked ever since.  That said, I think...generally speaking...Linux will grow within a generation of people.  It's just a matter of time.  MS is so entrenched, I can't imagine them ever going away, so I think it will evolve into what will at first seem like a reluctant marriage between the two, and later into a working relationship.

---

### Post by comfortablynumb on 2007-11-07
I just read through all 60 some pages today, I been trying to switch to Ubuntu completely since the 7.10 release. 

As for Firefox "greying out" I can't recall if you stated what version of Firefox your running. I know the latest stable Ubuntu version is 2.0.0.8 and the latest from Mozilla is 2.0.0.9 which should fix some of the bugs going on. On my other computer when it did the upgrade to 2.0.0.8 I was getting that "greying out" I disabled Compiz and Firefox ran fine after that. I unfortunately broke X and just reinstalled Ubuntu (having a seperate /home was the best advice I've seen for installs! ) I haven't bothered with the Restricted Nvidia drivers and all that to try and get the eye candy back on yet.

**On my laptop IBM T23 I haven't found a way to get Compiz to work at all, I guess the graphics are to antiquated (Savage S3) **

I've yet to to find a way to get the Ubuntu Live Cd to boot on my Athlon 64 Box, I would do the alternate disc but I don't want to wipe that drive yet. 64 just goes to a black screen 32 bit goes to a screen with fast moving multicolor bars.

**But all in all I'm very impressed with 7.10, It's very stable, very fast and highly customizable **

**Ubuntu Computer specs**:
Duron 1300
Crucial PC3200 512MB DDR
ECS Mobo KT266 Chip
Nvidia Geforce II GTS

**Athlon 64 Box**
Geforce 6200 PCI Express
DFI Lanparty Nforce 4 mobo
AMD 64 3200 (939)
1 Gig Corsair DDR

### Post by Rmantingh on 2007-11-08
If you have to rely on Windows/IE compatibility then I think it might be safer
to use an emulator like VirtualBox to run a 'proper' version of Windows (or
dual-boot). Me personally, I would not trust Wine to do the job properly.
I'm running W2K in a VirtualBox VM to run such apps as BusinessObjects,
PLSQL Developer, ViceVersa, and MS Office and it runs very fast indeed.
I know that this does not allow you to make a clean break with Windows but
as you yourself hinted at the start of your thread there may always be a
need to retain Windows to allow certain corporate software to run that's
not available (yet) on Linux.

Rmantingh

### Post by darkness5723 on 2007-11-08
Alright.. I just read all 63 pages in one sitting (had nothing better to do) and I have to give you major props Professor Fate. I have not had nearly the experience you have had with Ubuntu, and I'm sure if I did I would have said screw it and gone to another distro. My Ubuntu is working almost flawlessly (except for the fact that Java isn't playing nice (but thats mostly my fault)).

Anyway.. if the greying of FF is still happening for you, it's definitely a compatibility with compiz-fusion issue. I had the same exact problem when I was running Gutsy 64-bit and Compiz-fusion. I know all of these solutions have been suggested before but just in case here's everything you should try before writing it off:

Upgrade FF using Ubuntuzilla. (should work)
Get alpha(or did it move to beta?) FF-3 either through repo's or build from source (just dont go bleeding edge).
Remove compiz-fusion (not sure if this will work because it's built into Gutsy now) or completely disable it.
Run feisty (easiest way, lol I've been using feisty with compiz for a while on my laptop and it's worked perfectly).

I've noticed that Java-intensive pages caused this to happen, because I had the same problem with Gutsy 64-bit on my desktop, but since I switched back to 32-bit it's been fine. 

Anyway, I think this bet is GREAT and would make a superb blog. Even if your friend loses I'm sure he got what he really wanted, for you to experience linux, and being a tech nerd like all of us here you know you love fixing the challenges!

---
