### Post by professor fate on 2007-11-08
Hi ya'll,

The past hour or so was very interesting.  I had to...RE INSTALL!  Yes, I think the computer had a bad reaction to either Wine or Flash 8.  I downloaded Crossover Office for Linux, but before installing that I wanted to uninstall Wine and Flash 8.  I first started with Flash.  I went Applications > Wine > Macromedia > Uninstall.  It said it was uninstalled, but it seemed to go pretty quick.  I wasn't convinced.  Next I uninstalled Wine through the Synaptic Package Manager.  After that the poop hit the fans.  Problems included...

1. When I started to type in the search bar on Google the cursor would take over and just give me periods...................................I couldn't stop it.  I had to hard reboot. 
2. If I tried to access the home directory from the menu bar it would take me to file system/var.  And...the Stop icon on the file browser would flash endlessly.  The only way to get out was to hard reboot.
3. I tried to recover some files, but when I stuck the USB stick in, it wouldn't detect.  

I really couldn't use the computer, so I had to reinstall.  LOL.

### Post by mini_g on 2007-11-09
> **professor fate said:**
> Hi ya'll,

The past hour or so was very interesting.  I had to...RE INSTALL!  Yes, I think the computer had a bad reaction to either Wine or Flash 8.  I downloaded Crossover Office for Linux, but before installing that I wanted to uninstall Wine and Flash 8.  I first started with Flash.  I went Applications > Wine > Macromedia > Uninstall.  It said it was uninstalled, but it seemed to go pretty quick.  I wasn't convinced.  Next I uninstalled Wine through the Synaptic Package Manager.  After that the poop hit the fans.  Problems included...

1. When I started to type in the search bar on Google the cursor would take over and just give me periods...................................I couldn't stop it.  I had to hard reboot. 
2. If I tried to access the home directory from the menu bar it would take me to file system/var.  And...the Stop icon on the file browser would flash endlessly.  The only way to get out was to hard reboot.
3. I tried to recover some files, but when I stuck the USB stick in, it wouldn't detect.  

I really couldn't use the computer, so I had to reinstall.  LOL.

You can delete the ~/.wine directory (this directory contains all of the programs that you've install under WINE, its registry, etc.  It's basically Windows-in-a-box ;) ) as a last resort (this gives you a "fresh" version of WINE to work with and does not affect other users) if your WINE setup is messed up.

To double-check the Flash uninstall, you could've gone to ~/.wine/drive_c/Program\ Files/?Macromedia?/...  to double-check.

As for the ultra-quick uninstall bit, yeah, I've noticed that myself at times (took like 1 to 4 seconds for removing a 4+ GB program recently on an PATA/IDE HDD).

And as a side note, I've never really trusted the uninstall icon.  I've normally pulled the command into terminal/CLi and ran it there, so as to make sure nothing went wrong.

(Also, I noticed near the beginning of your bet, that you had troubles with the Compiz window decorator.  I'm not sure if you've found this yet, but hitting Alt+F2 and typing in "metacity --replace" will get the Gnome WM back, or for Compiz "compiz --replace".)

---

### Post by suupaabaka on 2007-11-10
A question to the Prof: Have you used/considered another Linux distribution? Maybe Gentoo would be right up your alley, allowing you to tailor each piece of software to your system. 

From what I understand, there's a longer waiting period as software installs (as it's compiled from source code) but you end up with exactly what *you* want, rather than what the developer putting together a package might have wanted. I've been considering putting Gentoo on this laptop, but after an ultimately failed 4-hour install session from a minimal CD, I'm putting it off. I'm sure the LiveCD will see more success, though.

Pluses for Ubuntu:
1. The repository setup and add/remove software are the bomb. It makes it very easy to select from a variety of software.
2. No anti-virus or spyware updates to contend with
3. Fast as snot on a waxed surfboard. This laptop is quick.
4. Bootup and wireless connection is very fast. I normally don't turn my computers off, but those are desktops.  Laptops are different in that respect.
5. Compiz is very nice.  I'm not into the cube stuff, but there are other nice features.  I don't know which one this is, but one I'm looking for I've seen on Youtube.  If one has many windows or folders open, they hit a button and then all the windows/folders get reduced in size and are spread out on the desktop.  

The downside:
The Crysis demo was released on the 26th and I can't play it. :(
I haven't looked into it, but does Ubuntu have a DX10 emulation for video drivers?  Crysis on a DX10 card is ungodly.  Don't know how many gamers we have here.

it should also work by placing your mouse in the upper right corner, but it does not seem to work on my laptop. :(

### Post by R_T_H on 2007-11-10
I have read this post avidly, and the conflicting ideologies intrigue me. I use both - I run Xubuntu on an old £20 laptop, and Vista on my main computer. Despite the fact I only got the laptop off a friend at the beginning of last week, and it was the first time I had *ever* used Linux, I've found it's relatively easy to migrate. Of course, some things irritate me because I am new and I don't understand them. I have no idea how to install OpenOffice, but AbiWord works fine. Some of the games are a bit odd and don't work all that well (see thread:[http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=608829](http://ubuntuforums.org/showthread.php?t=608829)), but most of it seems fine.

However, on my main machine I prefer Vista, because it looks cool, I understand it and my siblings and parents understand it so I don't have to explain something every 5 minutes. Some people claim it is bugged, but I've had **no** problems with it (at first I didn't know about about UAC, but after learning about it I've been fine).

Windows may be evil, but you've got to admire them for it. And if you're a retard (like me :D), most of their software is easy to use, and after a while it is second nature. I think Linux is best for those who have a specific purpose in mind, such as running on a poor system (which is why I use Xubuntu on my £20 laptop).

I think the most viable solution is not one based on ideologies, but on practicalities.

### Post by Count Doofus on 2007-11-10
Vista is what made me switch to a linux build. Infact I would compare gusty gibbon with windows 2000 because it was about the time when the whole windows OS became much more robust and much more controllable while reamining easy to to use for the average mullet user from an admin point of view. Linux has on the other hand always been stable, secure, manageble while remaining an enigma to the average mullet. Now that users can install aps without having to tinker with the command line for hours while gaining much insight into pci bus architecture. The only thing that is still holding back linux is the documentation and nomeclature which is either really cryptic and assumes that the reader knows details concerning PC architecture or the nomenclature isn't used in used in windows and there fore the instructions are not understood either. The product linux is great at this point in the game but our documentation of how to use it relies on the user know someone with much linux experience and that is a big step to overcome. Trust me I'd like nothing better than every user in my company to be sporting  a gusty,open office, and mySQL lap or desktop issue so they would have less opportunity to tear up their system whenever they like.

well that's my rant !:confused:

### Post by stijngysemans on 2007-11-11
Concerning the flash problem.

Suppose you are sharing a .fla file between two computers. If you open you .fla file on a second computer which doesn't have all the proper fonts installed, normally flash would pop-up a dialog asking for a replacement font. 
Did it ask you that this time?

What you can do is change the font to a well know ubuntu font. Afterwards, i'm sure that the flash compiler embeds the font in the swf file. There are some options when rendering the font to do this: select the text field and choose "Embed" on the property pane. 

You can also choose to embed a font using Actionscript

You can find more information here.
[http://www.adobe.com/devnet/flash/quickstart/embedding_fonts/](http://www.adobe.com/devnet/flash/quickstart/embedding_fonts/)

I have been developing flash applications since flash 4, only on windows though. I'm sure we can tackle this problem as well!

---

### Post by hollymcr on 2007-11-11
I've just spent 3hrs reading this whole thread - fascinating and (as others have said) full credit to the Prof for persevering. A few quick catch-up comments:

Firefox "going grey": I have this problem since upgrading to 7.10 (from 7.04, both 64-bit). I've found that the FF3 betas don't have the problem (and you don't need to uninstall the existing FF to find out). This does seem to be an Ubuntu bug rather than a FF one from what I've read, hopefully it'll be fixed soon.

Wine: I'd recommend using current versions rather than those bundled with Ubuntu; Wine is a fast moving target but they build their own packages for Ubuntu: [http://www.winehq.org/site/download-deb](http://www.winehq.org/site/download-deb) Even better: when adding repos this way you get all the advantages of automatic updates as they become available, so staying current is easy (assuming you want to: it's easy to disable if you don't).

IE on Wine: IE6 installs just fine on Wine. IE7's rendering engine will also install (see scripts here: [http://www.tatanka.com.br/ies4linux/page/Main_Page](http://www.tatanka.com.br/ies4linux/page/Main_Page)) which means you can test IE6 and IE7 on the same machine. (The full IE7 won't currently install so you get IE6 with it's rendering replaced by IE7 - no tabs etc but you see what the page will look like in IE7.) At work I'm the only one with a Linux desktop, and I'm alsp the only one that can test IE7 websites. Go figure!

Tahoma font: This is a font that a lot of Windows apps seem to use so it's not uncommon to find you need it even if you're not using it. Similarly I've found some Windows apps won't run reliably under Wine unless I first install IE6.

Codecs and stuff: I haven't seen anyone mention automatix ([www.getautomatix.com);](www.getautomatix.com);) that would have been an easier way to install a lot of the stuff you've installed so far.

FitDay: Do the comments on Wine's app database: [http://www.linux.com/articles/51606](http://www.linux.com/articles/51606) match your experiences? Also, I found this article about fitness apps for Linux: [http://www.linux.com/articles/51606](http://www.linux.com/articles/51606) I note that FitDay can be used online, but I assume you're looking for some of the extra features in the desktop app?

Flash 8 on Wine: Again the Wine app database page has some useful info: [http://appdb.winehq.org/objectManager.php?sClass=version&iId=3673](http://appdb.winehq.org/objectManager.php?sClass=version&iId=3673) There's a lot of info there that might go over your head at this point (but then at the rate you're progressing probably not!) but shout here if anything looks helpful but you need assistance with. Some of this stuff you'll probably find you can avoid a few re-installs if you ask first rather than just trying stuff (not that I would be any different).

Virtual machines: VirtualBox etc will be your "get out of jail free" card if you decide to move to Linux full time. I know you can't use them under the terms of your bet, but personally they've been a major contributor to my ability to switch, 

Should any of this be this hard? No, but when you consider how much is possible *without* the support of the vendors imaging what it would be like if they helped! How far do you think you'd get with Flash 8 on Windows if Macromedia only supported the Mac? That's not supposed to be a counterargument to any implied criticism as there hasn't been any to counter! But when you look at the size of MS and then think that once you get Vista installed you still rely on many other companies to provide drivers, applications, etc to make Vista "useful", the fact that so much can be achieved without widespread industry support is, frankly, incredible.

PS: You haven't mentioned stuff like email: are you collecting email on Linux? What are you using?

---

### Post by Tasogare_Mike on 2007-11-11
I skimmed over this thread so excuse me if I missed something...

This is a very subjective bet IMO. Although this has been a very informative and entertaining thread, I think that it is unreasonable to expect Ubuntu to run all file types, codecs, (even games) etc., because of the proprietary nature of them.

I think that as long as you can perform the equivalent task in Ubuntu then it would be fair.
You stated, "IF there is anything I can’t do on Ubuntu [...] that I can do on Vista"
For example, you can type a document with OO Writer; and you can type a doc with WinWord. You can view DVDs with VLC; and you can view them with WMP.  

If you were to (hypothetically) say that you couldn't play a movie using proprietary formats (made for Vista, maybe a new format known as .wmvx) in Ubuntu and say that Ubuntu failed, then I think this bet is BS (eventhough the community will eventually find a way to play it ;-) ).

But I won't tell your friend that ;-)

Also I don't just think it's subjective, but unbalanced in general.  Maybe the title of the thread should be versed like a sporting event: "Ubuntu @ Windows"
I say this because this seems like a seige on Windows that Ubuntu is having trouble with.
This is Ubuntu trying to get into your Windows-laced world. It is easier to defend then attack.  I guess I'm having trouble expressing myself here, so I hope I'm getting at least part of my point across here.

P.S. At any rate I think this is a great thread. I'll finish reading it when I have time.
Please update the OP to let us know how you're leaning with regard to switching in the future.  As one poster said maybe a percentage scale or something...    Thanks.

1) Just jump in and see what happens

2) find someone with your computer and ask them how it went

3) do some research on the hardware in your computer. this can be as in depth or superficial as you like. At least find out if things like your internet card, sound, monitor are supported. A place to check this out might be [http://www.dohickey-project.com/](http://www.dohickey-project.com/)

---

### Post by satx on 2007-11-12
Well, Ubuntu Community, we have a responsibility to assist Professor Fate with any issues he might have. While the learning curve is not too steep, it is for those who have never used UNIX/LINUX. 

I first learned UNIX in the mid-80's, then suffered through the Microsoft concepts of an OS. MS continues to add bloated code upon bloated code, requiring more and more RAM/HDD space to accommodate its inefficient coding.

Imagine my joy when I made the plunge to try Ubuntu and found it amazingly simple to install and use. I am currently running WIN XP/Ubuntu 7.10 dual boot HP d4650y box w/4GB RAM, 2 X 2.4 GHz Dual Core Intel CPU. Ubuntu runs blazingly fast. I just installed CompizFusion- WOW! What effects. I am using Open Office- that works great as well- seamless import of all MS Office products.

Just because I can, I am running WIN XP under VMWARE in my Ubuntu partition. I use SAMBA and SMB4K and I can transfer files seamlessly between all partitions and another HP 4650y. 

I rarely use XP at home anymore. I am having to much fun w/UBUNTU. I have everything I need. If I need something, I go to the repositories. What a collection of programs.

Professor Fate, we are with you! Good luck. I think you will find Ubuntu far superior to Vista.

### Post by hollymcr on 2007-11-12
> **R_T_H said:**
> I'm not sure about virtual machines - I'm switching to Linux at the moment, and I've found the best thing is to jump in. It makes it a steeper learning curve, but when things go right, it's more enjoyable. 

I agree with this completely, but I still find VirtualBox etc a useful backup. I rarely use them - in fact I'm surprised how little I use them given how Windows centered I thought my job was. However, if I have an issue for a client that I can't resolve in Linux (because I need to run some Windows software they use to resolve it) I need the ability to do that. 

Actually I find many Windows apps run better in virtual machines than on real machines because it's a lot easier to prevent the machines getting cluttered with rubbish, and I can have different virtual machines for different purposes.

### Post by hollymcr on 2007-11-13
> **satx said:**
> You try installing NVIDIA drivers in VMWARE?

This won't work as your virtual machine does not have an Nvidia card in it. Your host machine might, but that's irrelevant; the virtual machine is precisely that - virtual.

That's why you need to install the VMWare drivers, and not mess with all the driver disks that come with your PC. It also means you can transfer the VM from one host to another with completely different hardware (and host OS) without affecting it.

The same is true with other VMs (eg VirtualBox).

---

### Post by Joedude on 2007-11-13
yes, he could use gparted to shrink his swap space then resize the adjacent partiion to fill the gap. I personally use 8Gb root, 1Gb SWAP, rest for home. If gparted will do any damage to your data through those operations, it will warn you. you can either open a terminal and type "sudo gparted" or, you may all ready have it as an option in administration. In that case you could just click on it.

### Post by brennydoogles on 2007-11-13
> **afderrick said:**
> So I've been keeping up with this for the past few days and must say it took me an entire afternoon and a little bit to initially read through everything.  I am very new to Ubuntu but have no bet going and am currently dual booting and there are certain things I just haven't moved over to doing in the Ubuntu simply because of lack of diskspace, I want to build a new PC with just Ubuntu OS.  

My question is this though, I have read about having the multiple partitions for root and home.  What are the advantages (why do it this way) and what size partitions would I have for each?  In Windows I have my OS, Program files, and personal documents all split up on different partitions and have enjoyed having it all spread out.

Probably not the right forum for this, but it was brought up and I was curious.

I recommend about 10 GB for root if you have it (although you can have a working install on 6 or 7), depending on ram about 1 GB swap, and the rest as home. The advantages of this include making it easier to do a fresh install instead of an upgrade every 6 months (I find this is easier in the long run), and the ability to easily keep all personal data in the same place if you decide to switch distros.

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-11-13
> **Frak said:**
> Then that is a normal swap size. Swap is 2 1/4x of your actual RAM.
That's the default configuration, but unless you're making custom live CDs all the time, you don't really need that much on top of 2 gigs of RAM. As I understand it, swap space above about 1 gig is more or less wasted.

### Post by Frak on 2007-11-13
> **professor fate said:**
> I'm curious.  This laptop claims to have 120GB (but probably closer to 110), so what would be the ideal partition arrangement and size?
No more than 20GB for the / partition (at the beginning of the drive), about a 1GB - 1.5GB Swap (End of drive, NOTE: You must make a logical partition *then *a swap partition, since swap partitions can't exist on primary partitions) then leave the rest for your /home partition right in the middle, i.e. the "sweet" spot. :)

---

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-11-13
Depends on where you're checking it from. If you're still in Gparted, it should tell you. If you want to check after you've reinstalled the OS, you can type:
```
df -h
```
to check the mounted partitions (home and root)
and 
```
free
```
to check the swap file.

---

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-11-13
Is it possible to post a screenshot? (i.e., do you have a camera handy -- I know you're in the installer; just wanna make sure I know where you are)

It's been a minute since I've done this, but I believe you right-click on that big free space thing, and select "make new partition." Then you enter the size (e.g., 10 GB), the filesystem (ext3) and the mount point ( / ). Rinse and repeat.

EDIT: I should add, too, that the root partition needs to be a *primary* partition. Be sure to select that option when it asks.

---

### Post by TheIrishGuy on 2007-11-13
go to manual partitioning

make a 15 gig (15000mb)  partition

set its mount point as /

make another partition using the rest of the space (leaving 1000mb at the end)

make its mount point as /home

make a swap partition on the free space

in the old days it was said make a swap double the size of your ram, but it aint really needed, also if you want to run everything in ram, adjust your "swappiness" to 0, this way it will try to put everything in ram rather than using the "page file" / "swap".

next time your reinstall format /

leave /home alone and it will saved your documents and settings folder ;)

---

### Post by professor fate on 2007-11-14
Sorry, no camera here.

I used the terminal on the live cd to get into Gparted.

Questions:

/  (primary, extended, logical)?
swap (the same)
/home (the same)

Also, should they all be ext3?

---

### Post by p_quarles on 2007-11-14
/  -- needs to be primary, formatted as ext3
swap  -- secondary, not formatted (a partition mounted as swap will automatically get it right)
/home  -- either, formatted as ext3

---

### Post by TheIrishGuy on 2007-11-14
why are you using the terminal lol? x wont start off the live CD?

sudo rm /etc/X11/xorg.conf
startx
it will load it up, it wont be beautifull but youll get a desktop :)

once xorg makes a log for the first time you can figure out some things then, as Xorg can be a pain sometimes to new users this is handy info

first off find the Vsync Hsync of your screen

```

cat /var/log/Xorg.0.log | grep PixClock
(II) fglrx(0): Ranges: V min: 56  V max: 75 Hz, H min: 31  H max: 80 kHz, PixClock max 140 MHz

```


```

cat /var/log/Xorg.0.log | grep "Image Size"
(II) fglrx(0): Max H-Image Size [cm]: horiz.: 38  vert.: 30
(II) fglrx(0): clock: 108.0 MHz   Image Size:  376 x 301 mm

```

The DPMS settings should be enough but if you feal like getting a modeline

```

cat /var/log/Xorg.0.log | grep "1280x1024"
(II) fglrx(0): 1280x1024@75Hz
(--) fglrx(0): Virtual size is 1280x1024 (pitch 0)
(**) fglrx(0): *Mode "1280x1024": 135.0 MHz (scaled from 0.0 MHz), 80.0 kHz, 75.0 Hz
(II) fglrx(0): Modeline "1280x1024"  135.00  1280 1296 1440 1688  1024 1025 1028 1066
(--) fglrx(0): Virtual size is 1280x1024 (pitch 1280)
(**) fglrx(0): *Mode "1280x1024": 135.0 MHz (scaled from 0.0 MHz), 80.0 kHz, 75.0 Hz
(II) fglrx(0): Modeline "1280x1024"  135.00  1280 1296 1440 1688  1024 1025 1028 1066

```

putting it all together to make a monitor section in your /etc/X11/xorg.conf

```

Section "Monitor"
	Identifier   "DELL E197FP"
	Option		"DPMS"
	HorizSync	30-81
	VertRefresh	56-76
	DisplaySize	376	301	# 1280x1024 96dpi
	Modeline "1280x1024"  135.00  1280 1296 1440 1688  1024 1025 1028 1066
EndSection

```

max res, max hertz

### Post by Centx on 2007-11-14
I've not read the 80 pages of responses, but AFAIK your root("/") partition can be whatever you like, as long as the GRUB boot stage 1, 1.5 or 2 (I don't remember which, google GRUB for more info) is within some kind of block or cycle size (very vague i know, but the point was that root("/") can be wherever you want.

About the X issue, isn't BulletProofX supposed to take care of the X-everywhere issue?

GL/HF, and if you want a good (native) game for linux, try out Enemy Territory: Quake Wars =)

### Post by gazj on 2007-11-14
> **Frak said:**
> No more than 20GB for the / partition (at the beginning of the drive), about a 1GB - 1.5GB Swap (End of drive, NOTE: You must make a logical partition *then *a swap partition, since swap partitions can't exist on primary partitions) then leave the rest for your /home partition right in the middle, i.e. the "sweet" spot. :)

swap partitions can exist on primary partitions

### Post by Joedude on 2007-11-14
maybe a little late, but here:

Click manual partition:
Enter the values;
1 ext3 10Gb
2 at the end of disk space 1 Gb Swap
3 remainder as ext 3

click next
select the 10 Gb ext3 and use the drop down box to indicate /
Swap should all ready be set
select the big partition and use the drop down box to inidcate /home

hit next and away you go...

One thing to keep in mind though is that Linux is a full seven course meal.  30 days of using only Linux is hard to do when you have no prior knowledge.  Luckily Linux has grown up these past two years.  We have made good progress on making first time users at home.  The years prior to the last two years were seen as techie turf with Linux zealots running things.  Now things have matured from the "live in your mother's basement and hack all day" to a market supported by products designed to satisfy the average person.

One could think of many things you would do day in and day out under windows but since you can use emulation software you should be good to go.  Let's hope the game is kept fair and square and you don't put arbitrary barriers in your own way due to the lack of knowledge.

### Post by grndslm on 2007-11-14
I **know** it's already been said, but it's an awesome bet.

It just shows how each OS has their place, and most anything can be done on the other with a little bit of work.  If you use vmware, you can still retain the beautiful Linux foundation, running the odd-ball apps that you must from time to time... or keep dual-booting for games if you must.  There are a few decent 3D games for Linux like Tremulous, Torcs, Neverball, Neverputt, Anagramarama, Armagetron, Chromium, Flubo Puyo, Xmoto, Sauerbraten, and so on and so forth... the best part about Debian-based distros like linux is that you can install all of those games with ONE single command after installation of the OS:

sudo aptitude install torcs neverball anagramarama armagetron chromium flobopuyo xmoto .....and so on and so forth  that way after the inevitable installation, you can have all your apps back in an hour or so, only having started from a single 700MB CD.

Things like Sauerbraten would have to be installed manually tho, which is prolly the toughest thing for Linux Noobs.   Download the program unp and you can unzip any packaging file without having to remember other commands like **tar zxvf gamefile.tar.gz**, then **cd** (not a new command for DOS users!) to the directory and run **./configure; make; make install**.  Sometimes you will have to read the README file, tho... but luckily, it is in English.

All I can say is that with a little work, Ubuntu rocks, Compiz-Fusion rocks, MythTV rocks, RockBox rocks, OpenWRT rocks, VMware rocks, and of course Linux Rocks!!

Thank you Richard Stallman, Linus Torvalds, and all the other open source programmers out there!!!

### Post by rallen71366 on 2007-11-14
First, let me say that Linux, Ubuntu flavored, has become my favorite OS over the last year. Unfortunately, the only problems that I've had with it are natural language recognition (as mentioned above), and professional level CAD/CAM software. 

G-code for CNC machines has to either be written by hand (long and prone to errors, trust me...) or generated by CAM software, a peculiar blend of 3-d CAD and motion control software. Some of the commercial software may run, at $17K a seat, but I really couldn't find anything opensoure that is comparable. 

There are several projects in the works trying to develope FOSS CAM, or even decent 3-d CAD, by building onto existing projects like Blender or POV Ray modellers, but none of them are quite reaching the bar yet. Which is sad, because good CAM software is the most expensive part of a CNC system. If it weren't for that, anyone could build a decent desktop prototyping system for a couple hundred (devalued) US dollars.

### Post by duality on 2007-11-14
Hi

Saw this thread on [digg.com]("http://digg.com/linux_unix/The_bet_is_on_Ubuntu_vs_Microsoft_Help_Ubuntu_win") and read a bit from it so i thought I'd add some info about how to go about doing things on Ubuntu.

First of all you should use the 'alternative' installer (Not the LiveCD) it says "Alternate install CD" when you choose the download location, it's much easier (except for the partitioning maybe), and more consistent.

When partitioning, you can use any folder as a partition, ex. if you can just set a partition to be mounted on /home/username/myfolder during install and that folder will be on a separate partition, you're going to need at least two partitions from the start, the '/' (root) and swap (which should be twice the size of your ram).

After installing it for the first time there's a big chance that you will have many problems with linux and change loads of things when trying to fix it until it becomes too hard to fix the problems because of all the changes made, so it's normal to reinstall alot of times until you know what you're doing, the solution is to get VMWARE SERVER (a Virual Machine which you probably have heard of or used) and install Ubuntu on it, that way you can try making any changes and just roll back the VM to a previous state if you make snapshots, that way you can try stuff and play around with linux freely and it's config files etc. without the fear of ruining your OS and wasting alot of time fixing it.
(3d graphics etc. wont work on vmware)
The software is also free.

Then for the software, most software will exist in the ubuntu repository and I strongly suggest that you have a look at this webpage: [http://ubuntuguide.org/wiki/Ubuntu:Gutsy](http://ubuntuguide.org/wiki/Ubuntu:Gutsy) that explains how to do alot of things and also how to install alternatives to windows software, how to add repositories etc. I think it's the most useful guide for people new to Ubuntu.

Things might get a bit tricky if you need to run a dual screen setup and you need to edit the xorg.conf file but I hope you find solutions to all that.

You should also know that Ubuntu has lots of services enabled by default so you might want to read a bit about what all of them are so you know your system and can optimize it quite a bit by disabling the ones that you don't need. There's also a good bash scripting guide which I think you can learn alot about your system by learning it or from just having a quick look on how it works: [http://tldp.org/LDP/abs/html/](http://tldp.org/LDP/abs/html/)


GL

---

### Post by gn0stik on 2007-11-14
In most industries I've worked in, Linux simply could not run, nor had any equivalent to the line of business applications. 

Now if your friend is saying "home desktop use only", with no games, he may have you. Those are pretty lame requirements, if you ask me. 

Last time I went on a linux kick, what turned me away was hardware support, however. I had an ATI video card, that ran beryl like crap, and a tv tuner that simply would not workright. I wanted to turn the box into a PVR / Entertainment PC. 

Now, granted, it was a tuner that I just had laying around, but it was supposedly supported, and I tried all the little hacks people suggested to get it working, the way it was supposed to, and it just didn't. It worked, in that some features of the device would work.. But many just weren't supported by the linux drivers. For the most part, I have found many linux drivers to not work as well as their windows counterparts.

The other thing, was banking. Sure there's gnucash, but it sucks. Gimme a quicken, or msmoney equivalent. 

One thing I noticed about the requirements your friend gave was that, he didn't mention anything about using virtualization, or emulation to accomplish things in linux, that you can do in windows.

Seems to me, that since he didn't mention those workarounds, you can just say, there's no Mapi Client. If you can't run outlook, or some other mapi client to attach to an exchange email server, you've got him. He says nothing about you running wine, or virtual box to run actual windows applications in linux. I could be wrong about the outlook thing now, it's been a while.. there may be something.

Last but not least... try writing a .net program on that box.... Ahem. But that's not playing fair.

### Post by sizzam on 2007-11-14
I didn't read this whole thread, but for a free laptop, here's something I do on Windows that I haven't figured out how to do on Linux:

keyboard macros for text replacement

I do technical support and have to type a lot of the same phrases over and over again.   In Windows, I use programs like [Shortkeys]("http://www.shortkeys.com/") or [AutoHotKey]("http://www.autohotkey.com/") to do text replacement.   For example, I'll type 'xxcn' and those programs will automatically replace that text with 'Please update this ticket if further action is required, blah blah blah, yadda yadda yadda'.

These programs work across all programs (browsers, text editors, word processors, etc.   

I bet you can't find a text replacement utility for keyboard macros that works across all applications in Linux.

### Post by gpsblake on 2007-11-14
> **Geekberg said:**
> Im not sure what your buddy expects?  Ubuntu can run or do anything Windows can, at the least in wine or with VMServer!  So how can you not win the bet?

Example.

I have a Garmin GPS. There are no USB drivers or software to use it so it is worthless in Linux.  I'm not blaming Linux for that though, it's simply that a lot of hardware manufactures don't write drivers for it.  Therefore I must have Windows installed on my computer. Lots of other hardware and software fall into this category. 

And yes, you have to concede the games to MS also, it's not the fault of Linux either. However, in order for great games to be part of Linux, it's going to have to come from commercial vendors, ie, not Free. This goes against what a lot of Linux users want but the truth is, for Linux to grow, For-Profit software companies are going to have to write profitable non-free software for Linux. Right now, there isn't a market for that. 

 **HOWEVER**, on the user end, that doesn't matter, to them, Linux doesn't work when there aren't drivers for your hardware devices.

### Post by einfeldt on 2007-11-14
The problem with this contest is that it is entirely subjective and the judge (the Microsoft guy) has a vested interest in the outcome.  There are no objective benchmarks.  I am an attorney, and so let's pretend that this case went to court.  How would either party to this contest demonstrate to the court that he had prevailed?

And yet the stakes are fairly high, a notebook.

I can tell you that if this case came into my law office, I would decline to take the case, simply because there was no meeting of the minds about the criteria for who deciding who won.  Put differently, there simply is no contract here, in my opinion, and thus no way to enforce it in a court of law.

Coincidentally, I tell you my bias.  I run Kubuntu and openSUSE in my law practice, and I am a volunteer level one sys admin for a public middle school in San Francisco.  So I would be inclined to say that Ubuntu is on relative feature parity with Windows, hence the Linux guy should win.  I don't see how a reasonable Windows user could disagree, given the parameters that these two parties have set:  no gaming.

### Post by vertexoflife on 2007-11-14
Okay. Well. I definitely just failed an assignment by slacking off all afternoon reading 89 pages. 

Prof-
I've watched your experience of Linux develop, and I see a sort of image of my experience. Trying then becoming slowly hooked. Now you've even taken to writing some new things!

I found this speech to be pure genius:

> Hello,

It's 5:45am here and I'm about to hop on the exercise bike and watch a video I downloaded using Deluge Bit torrent. I think overall Ubuntu, and I imagine this can probably be said for many Linux distros, is a very fine OS. Undoubtedly it's number one strength is its ability to be customized to the n'th degree. I've looked at many many posts here whereby users have submitted desktop screen shots of their systems and have been amazed by the variety.

Oddly though, I think Ubuntu's greatest strength is also its greatest weakness, BUT...I should preface this by saying it depends on the type of user. Let me put it this way. Vista is like canned soup. You know exactly what you're going to get, and to a large degree the soup will be safe for consumption. Ubuntu, or Linux in general, is like getting all of the ingredients for soup, but it's up to you to make it. One can customize every little nuance to affect the taste, and because of this one can experience a flavor of the day if they will. The former soup requires less imagination, but it's generally safe because it has been finely measured, processed, and canned. The latter requires more imagination and possible risks for those that don't know the first thing about cooking, but has a potential of producing something extraordinary. A good cook (e.g., someone computer savvy or at least an interest in it) is a great candidate for Linux. A not so good cook (e.g., a "I just want it to work" person) is not a good candidate for Linux simply because they're more interested in....and this is the important part...eating, and not so much about what they're eating or how it's made.

I'm in my early 40s. Most of my friends my age have little interest in tackling an OS. My younger friends (e.g., the guy I have the bet with) are more computer savvy and like to tinker with this stuff. I've always been that way though too. I was into computers in grade school, and have been hooked ever since. That said, I think...generally speaking...Linux will grow within a generation of people. It's just a matter of time. MS is so entrenched, I can't imagine them ever going away, so I think it will evolve into what will at first seem like a reluctant marriage between the two, and later into a working relationship.

Thank you for trying Ubuntu, and even contributing back to the cummunity with the write-up on your sound-card. I think you're one of the most decent people I have read about or heard from ina very long time, and I am excited that you got to have this experience.

---

### Post by z0M6 on 2007-11-14
Hey Prof. Fate.

Interesting read this thread. Skipped some parts though.

Anyways I figured that I should register and help out by answering some of your questions. Some of them I didn't really see a good answer for, but I did skip some parts so maybe I just missed it.

About naming your partitions:
As far as i know gparted does not have the option to label your partitions with custom names. qtparted does however. Too bad you don't have that one available during the install of Ubuntu.

I think that kubuntu has this available, but this becomes a question of choice of DE. Surely other threads for that.

I'm sure you have some sort of understanding of how partitions work by now, but in short:
Your hard drive is represented in Linux as a device name available in the /dev folder. From earlier in this thread I know that it is named /dev/sda

This is your hard drive. Note that other devices such at flash drives, cd-roms, external hard drives will be represented here.

The next thing is that each partition will also have a name. The first partition on your hard drive wil be named /dev/sda1. The second will be /dev/sda2. This is a modified version of it as I'll leave the use of primary and extended partitions for later discovery. I don think tha Ubuntu puts your swap in an extended partition as /dev/sda5

So you wanna name em'. I don't know if you can name an existing partition just like that. You can however do it while you make the partitions. (Not in the installer though.) I like to make my partitions manually. Not something that can be recommended to the beginner, but surely we would like to know.

First of is the partitioning of the hard drive.

(Assuming you have booted up your livecd because this will remove everything you have on this machine)

Lets get that console up and running:
Alt + F2, Terminal

Get some administrator rights:
```
sudo su -
```

Partition the hard drive:
```
cfdisk /dev/sda
```

First lets remove everything ^^
1. delete everything
2. make partitions after desired pattern (I go about this quite differently than everybody else so from earlier It would be something like partition 1 should be 10GB, partition 2 should be the amount you would want to use as swap. I use 1.15*Ram, yet I never seem to use any of it. Partition 3 should be the rest that I think you can use as your home folder. when you get the hang of things I sure you realize that you want to do this in a different way.)
3. make partition 1 bootable and shift the partition type of your swap partition to 82
4. write and quit.

Next you need to format them. I recommend using ext3.winedb
root partition:
```
mke2fs -j -L NameOfPartiton /dev/sda1
```
do the same for the home partition and you can leave the swap to the installer (although 'mkswap /dev/sda2' should do it)
The -j option tells it to use journaling. keeping a journal of where things are located.
The -L option uses the next argument for labeling your partition.

The tricky part is telling the installer to use what you just did, but I don't use ubuntu for reasons mentioned in my signature so I think someone else should answer this.

Flash 8:
I have no/little experience with this. But when the application gets platinum rating on [http://appdb.winehq.org/](http://appdb.winehq.org/) then I can pretty much guarantee that it works. I'm not completly sure about the fonts and how to best do it but I think installing the font on your system first and then making a symbolic link to the font in the font directory used by wine:
cd ~/.wine/drive_c/windows/fonts/
then sym-link:
ln -s "wherever gnome/ubuntu puts fonts"
Also, you can get the microsoft fonts on your system because microsoft released them freely. I'm sure a quick search on these forums will show how. They are called msttcorefonts I think.

You figured out the sound so nothing to mention there.

This ended up being a lengthy post and you might know alot of this by now so I hope some of it was usefull at least.

One last tip would be to check out what people has to say about different windows apps on [http://appdb.winehq.org/](http://appdb.winehq.org/) you should be able to figure out just about anything just by reading what other people have already asked there (as well as here).

---

### Post by professor fate on 2007-11-14
> **einfeldt said:**
> The problem with this contest is that it is entirely subjective and the judge (the Microsoft guy) has a vested interest in the outcome.  There are no objective benchmarks.  I am an attorney, and so let's pretend that this case went to court.  How would either party to this contest demonstrate to the court that he had prevailed?

And yet the stakes are fairly high, a notebook.

I can tell you that if this case came into my law office, I would decline to take the case, simply because there was no meeting of the minds about the criteria for who deciding who won.  Put differently, there simply is no contract here, in my opinion, and thus no way to enforce it in a court of law.

Coincidentally, I tell you my bias.  I run Kubuntu and openSUSE in my law practice, and I am a volunteer level one sys admin for a public middle school in San Francisco.  So I would be inclined to say that Ubuntu is on relative feature parity with Windows, hence the Linux guy should win.  I don't see how a reasonable Windows user could disagree, given the parameters that these two parties have set:  no gaming.

"I don't see how a reasonable Windows user could disagree"

Now that was a subjective statement. :)

There are benchmarks and they are not entirely subjective.  For instance, FitDay simply does not work in Wine or CX office.  It freezes up.  That's not a subjective opinion.  It's simply a fact.  (If you know of one that's open source with all the same features, please let me know).  The bench mark is that I can be as productive using open source apps or Microsoft apps using Wine or some other "like" emulation software.  This doesn't apply to VMserver because that's simply one OS in side of another.  I am using Open Office because it serves my needs.  I'm sure if I really pushed the envelope I could find a number of features in Office 2007 that Open Office can't do, but that's not reasonable because I don't normally use such exotic features in my daily computer use.  Thanks to many of the participants of this BB I've been introduced to a number of great open source apps that fulfil my needs.  At the same time there are a few MS platform products that I use frequently that have to work, and work reliably.  That's not asking for much.  

I've really enjoyed this entire experience in spite of the problems that have crept up.  The people here are fantastic.  This is easily one of the best BB communities I've ever been to.

Install the compiz configuration manager and have your windows maximize, minimize and open with a fade in like in vista. Right now I even have icons that resemble vista's. I like vista, but it's looks will get old, while i can change ubuntus look as often as i change my underwear.

You can do this with windows too. It's just not as popular due to the fact that on the windows platform, it's a pay product. Window blinds, and products of the ilk have been around a long time allowing you to skin windows to your hearts content. With it being a pay product, however, people are less likely to go for all the bling when it costs them a few bucks. Hell, now there's even a product called cube desktop that brings the cube effect, a flip desktop switcher, and  expose like app switcher to windows. with 6 desktops, not just 4, like beryl. They are planning to implement window effects, etc as well. So wobbly windows, and transition effects like fire will be there just like in beryl/compiz. Again, it's a pay product, so it won't be as popular, but the ability is there. So if you get bored enough with the appearance to pay for a new look, you can.

I think the most important thing is that even if you choose to stay with vista, you must come back and try out 8.04 and 8.10 etc. etc. With friends who initially didn't want to even try ubuntu (this is since 5.10) each time they see the next 6 monthly release they are impressed. If you use ubuntu every day the next release can seem massive. 

YOU DO NOT NEED TO READ BELOW, I KIND OF GOT CARRIED AWAY.

For example when I began using ubuntu, rhythmbox could do barely anything with my ipod, each release that has improved.

Gnome gets improved every 6 months and so do many gnome apps (like rhythmbox just mentioned) Gnome 2.20 has been the most pleasant upgrade for me, I can't explain why but just so many little features were improved.

Gimps development seems rather slow but the 2.4 was a very nice improvement. I love gimp, I have issues with it too, but if you don't look at it as a photoshop replacement (which it is for me) but just an image editor you realise that if it isn't better than photoshop, it IS the very next best thing. Others may disagree but I have used many editors over the last 5 years (mainly photoshop) and gimp just resonates with me.

For me open office is great. I am not a hardcore office user, I used to use it more at school but the occasional essay is basically all I need it for. For most students I can see no reason why they would need anything else! 

Pidgin is cool too :P etc. etc.

### Post by sicofante on 2007-11-14
Admins: please stop the flood. This has been a very interesting thread before it got exposure via Digg, Slashdot or whatever. Maybe requiring a 15 day membership before posting or a similar measure might stop the tourists?

On a different note, it's funny reading Linux can't do pro stuff in the audiovisual market. I've been in the computer graphics industry for some 20 years now. In the beginning we used Silicon Graphics hardware and software designed only for the IRIX platform. Every high-end app was developed there. Then one fine day Microsoft acquired Softimage (only to sell it back when the marketing was done) and a migration slowly took place, but we never quite liked MS products and the MS version of the software was a bad port for a long time. Finally SGI declined and software was developed for Windows first, then eventually ported. But no one ever really liked MS (I guess it was mostly about not liking to change for no technical reason, not because MS was that bad), and in the last few years the situation is reversing, thanks to Linux, and the trend is clearly leaving MS behind (probably for good). Although to be fair I must say Ubuntu is not the flavour usually chosen (Redhat/Fedora is) and Mac OS has gained a lot of popularity too. Sure, small shops still use Windows because low-end software runs only there, but you won't find many Windows systems in bigger studios.

Again, I'm not blaming Linux for this but for the end user, these are reasons why Linux won't take off with the general public.

### Post by jimbo99 on 2007-11-14
> **Crashmaxx said:**
> First of all, you guys are pretty rich to bet a couple thousand dollars on something like this. Second, I know what he is doing here, I would bet that you will win, but you will like Ubuntu so much after 30 days that you will use it most of the time. Probably the first thing you do with your new laptop will be install Ubuntu on it.

Anyway, I'm definitely *subscribed* to this thread. You should write a blog or something when you are done.



As has been said, you need to install the Adobe version of flash, it is very stable in comparison. You can find it in add/remove even after you have enabled universe repos.

Sound is tricky, but this is probably the best place to start:
[https://help.ubuntu.com/community/SoundTroubleshooting?highlight=%28sound%29](https://help.ubuntu.com/community/SoundTroubleshooting?highlight=%28sound%29)

The CPU usage I would bet is something new included in Gutsy. They included a fancy new desktop search function for some reason. It wants to index everything on your computer and can take up a lot of CPU. If you don't use it, then I recommend removing it. Just go into add/remove and find it and remove it. The package is called tracker, but it may be called "Desktop Search" or something else 'nicer' in add/remove. I don't know why they felt this should be included by default, but removing it is easy, so its not a big deal.

As for you new and most critical problem, I've never heard of something so strange. I'll keep looking for a solution.

He can also just download it from Adobe's site, just like he would under windows, and install the file from the download.  It is pretty simple. Run it, tell it where your browser is located, and answer yes to the question.  When asked to install to another place, answer no.  Restart FF and he's good to go.  Under Linux FF is extremely stable.  In the past couple years there have been only a few quirks that I have encountered and those have mostly been ironed out.  Part of the issue comes from them wanting a common code base for all platforms.  I understand they are going to make a versions specific to the OS now.  Why not?  They have been collecting the money (I understand they have like 30 million dollars banked from various partnerships) for some time so they should invest in ensuring the specific OSes get a version tailored to them.

### Post by jimbo99 on 2007-11-15
> **gn0stik said:**
> ... 

Last time I went on a linux kick, what turned me away was hardware support, however. I had an ATI video card, that ran beryl like crap,....

That was the fault of ATI not implementing the AIGLX feature that nVidia had available for months and months.  It is in no way a linux defect since ATI has supported binary closed drivers for a long time under Linux.  It is simply that they resisted or failed to implement it thus you had to work around the issue.

Anyway, as of now they supposedly have AIGLX support in their closed binary drivers for Linux.

### Post by unoobu on 2007-11-15
> **professor fate said:**
> [SIZE="7"]Cha Ching![/SIZE]Well...sort of.  I have sound out of the headphone port.  I never had that before.

I downloaded the 64bit version and threw it on.

Getting sound to work

    * Look up ALSA options
    * Add the following line to /etc/modprobe.d/alsa-base
      options snd-hda-intel probe_mask=1 model=3stack

[SIZE="6"]Ask him how to competently do the following on Ubuntu:[/SIZE]

1. Edit Video Footage COMPETENTLY
2. Do a chroma key effect in video footage.
3. Render a 3D animated model with alpha layer intact for compositing
4. Convert a video file to HD format
5. Burn a video file to a Blu Ray, or HDDVD with menus
6. Create an interactive DVD menu

8. Record audio on a professional level- i.e. Pro Tools, or Sonar
9. Edit HD Video Footage
10. Burn an Audio DVD

### Post by JGJones on 2007-11-15
Erm, as pointed out - these are high end solutions...granted it might be difficult but I thought I would point out that Linux have been used in high end solutions for films for example CinePaint have been used in Hollywood blockbusters such as The Last Samurai, Harry Potter and so on.

Granted not a movie editor, but it's a tool to work on movies.

However it's not impossible to make a movie on Linux. Elephant Dreams is a very high quality movie done using all FOSS tools and is available from - [http://www.elephantsdream.org/](http://www.elephantsdream.org/) (for example, all 3D effects was done in Blender).

But again all of that is high end solution. In my case - I have a vlog. I want something simple for my vlog - [http://vlog.gwallgofi.com](http://vlog.gwallgofi.com).

This means I need to be able to get film off a camcorder, edit it a bit and some simple effects. For this I use Kdenlive (you can find it in Add/Remove software, under all applications) which is a simple movie editor with some effects, timeline etc - it's really simple to use and quite decent for a simple editor.

PiTiVi is another movie editor but it's still horribly early in development but one I am keeping an eye on. Granted I've not tested HD video footage on these editors - but in my case why would I? My vlog videos are 640x480 in size...that's all I need, I don't need HD for a vlog website.

I'm deaf so I won't comment on anything audio-related as I have zero need of them. Hope that helps you.

EDIT - Forgot to include this link - [http://linuxmovies.org/software.html](http://linuxmovies.org/software.html) - it's a bit old, but does contain a list of various software on Linux that can be used to edit/create/touch up movies/sound etc - might be of use for anyone that is looking for it.

### Post by SniperSlap on 2007-11-15
I've managed to convince a friend of mine who despite being prone to using Windows has never been that big on it.  I came over to his house the other day and saw that he was still running Ubuntu.  I was like "You're still running Ubuntu?!"
He said "hell yeah, it's awesome"

While I've showed him World of Warcraft running AMAZINGLY via WINE already, he won't be able to do the same until his next laptop because he has a very low budget ATI card.  Hopefully they make some better drivers so that he can switch over completely.  I was really impressed.

Can Ubuntu "do what I do in Windows" - I guess so long as what you're doing isn't something designed to require Windows is the obvious answer.
Using Ubuntu is an opportunity to finally break free of standards that force you to pay for a software package with every different file format or standard of information exchange you encounter.
It is your freedom to use Ubuntu or not.  But remember - using Ubuntu is expressing your freedom in such a way that you're saying you don't want it taken away in the near future.

I am MORE productive and much more enlightened through my use of Ubuntu.  I also feel much better because I am ensuring that I contribute my experiences and encounters back to the community.

OSX?  You know, if you studied the OS carefully, you'd find out that it is very poorly designed (see pthreads and also the graphical APIs).  In all benchmarks, Linux outperforms.  That is not to say that OSX isn't a viable alternative to Windows - but if you're making the change, why not go all the way and use Ubuntu?

Simply put, the increase in users has already forced industry to start recognizing the OS.  Further, your use will most assuredly further that cause and trust me - you're not the only person making the switch!

Have some fun and bring the appeal back into computing while taking back your freedoms.

### Post by Rmantingh on 2007-11-15
Hi Prof,

I'm not sure if you still have problems with FitDay but I got it to work (I think).
I've downloaded an 'evaluation copy' of FitDay (version 1.0.0.6) and the latest
Wine package (0.9.49) from winehq.

1) Installed latest Wine (0.9.49)
2) In Wine Configuration, added an audio driver (Alsa) and set the Default Settings
   in Applications to Windows XP.
3) Installed FitDay
4) Ran FitDay (see screenshots).

I haven't tested it extensively and only added some food + activities
but all that seemed to work OK. I didn't get any freezes. Data entry is
a bit peculiar though: the dropdowns don't work until you type in at
least one character but perhaps that's normal, I don't know.

My system is Ubuntu Gutsy 7.10 AMD64.

### Post by Achetar on 2007-11-15
> **SniperSlap said:**
> I've managed to convince a friend of mine who despite being prone to using Windows has never been that big on it.  I came over to his house the other day and saw that he was still running Ubuntu.  I was like "You're still running Ubuntu?!"
He said "hell yeah, it's awesome"

While I've showed him World of Warcraft running AMAZINGLY via WINE already, he won't be able to do the same until his next laptop because he has a very low budget ATI card.  Hopefully they make some better drivers so that he can switch over completely.  I was really impressed.

Can Ubuntu "do what I do in Windows" - I guess so long as what you're doing isn't something designed to require Windows is the obvious answer.
Using Ubuntu is an opportunity to finally break free of standards that force you to pay for a software package with every different file format or standard of information exchange you encounter.
It is your freedom to use Ubuntu or not.  But remember - using Ubuntu is expressing your freedom in such a way that you're saying you don't want it taken away in the near future.

I am MORE productive and much more enlightened through my use of Ubuntu.  I also feel much better because I am ensuring that I contribute my experiences and encounters back to the community.

OSX?  You know, if you studied the OS carefully, you'd find out that it is very poorly designed (see pthreads and also the graphical APIs).  In all benchmarks, Linux outperforms.  That is not to say that OSX isn't a viable alternative to Windows - but if you're making the change, why not go all the way and use Ubuntu?

Simply put, the increase in users has already forced industry to start recognizing the OS.  Further, your use will most assuredly further that cause and trust me - you're not the only person making the switch!

Have some fun and bring the appeal back into computing while taking back your freedoms.

I totally agree with you (SniperSlap)! :KS :KS
As well as I believe that if you (professor fate) spent time learning the ins and outs of Ubuntu, you would want to buy yourself a new laptop just cuz your old one was contaminated by a Windows install!! (ok maybe thats a bit of a stretch...)
I am using VirtualBOX to run WinXP and FreeDOS right now, it rocks! 


P.S. the only reason I am using VirtualBOX in the first place is:
a. My mom won't let me scrap Win just yet, i'm still a minor :(
b. Command & Conquer: First Decade won't install under WINE, and won't detect game DVD if extracted, will install in VirtualBOX.
c. I use DOS for games like LEMMINGS that refuse to run under DOSBox. 

P.P.S. I feel sorry that you made a stupid bet like that and will have to buy your friend a new laptop!

### Post by matthewcraig on 2007-11-15
Just so you understand some people's negativity toward your bet... You aren't the first person to say, "I will try Ubuntu, but I bet it stinks."  The analysis is generally the same: "Yep, I knew it.  It stinks.  I am so happy to go back to Windows."  If you are a dyed-in-the-wool Windows fan, then you will not like anything else because you have already decided nothing is better.  Glad you're taking a look at Ubuntu, but you should try understanding why Ubuntu is better then Windows before comparing feature-to-feature.
